<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 18 May 2026 to 22 May 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

US macro deteriorated into a policy-constrained stagflationary bifurcation tape. Growth was not recessionary: housing showed a demand floor through NAHB and pending sales, LEI bounced, and claims stayed contained. But the quality of activity weakened: single-family housing deteriorated beneath aggregate construction beats, mortgage rates re-tightened, Philly Fed corrected sharply, services PMI barely expanded, and S&P manufacturing strength looked flattered by inventory / supply mechanics rather than clean final demand.

The decisive weekly shift came from the inflation-expectations and policy-constraint side. PMI / regional survey price pressure, tighter mortgage transmission and the University of Michigan final shock moved the regime away from simple soft-landing resilience. Labor remained the anchor preventing a recession call, but the 5-year UMich inflation-expectations rise turned consumer weakness into a Fed constraint rather than a clean dovish growth signal. The Fed speaker tape then institutionalized that regime: the April FOMC minutes, Waller’s Frankfurt pivot and Warsh’s handover all shifted the reaction function away from “next move = cut” toward hold-or-hike optionality. The final regime is not “growth collapse”; it is cost-push pressure, weakening demand quality, stable layoffs and reduced policy flexibility.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
Housing was mixed rather than cleanly negative: NAHB beat and pending sales improved for a second month, but MBA mortgage rates rose to 6.56%, single-family permits and starts weakened, and the aggregate construction beat was carried by multi-family. Business surveys deteriorated in quality: Philly Fed fell from 26.7 to -0.4, services PMI was barely expansionary at 50.9, and composite PMI did not accelerate. LEI rose 0.1%, but the six-month trend remained negative, leaving forward growth fragile.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Initial claims remained low at 209k, the four-week average declined to 202.5k, and continuing claims stayed contained below 1.8M. PMI employment softness and Philly employment weakness point to hiring caution, but claims measure separations and did not validate a labor-market break. Labor therefore remains a stabilizing anchor rather than a recession trigger.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
The inflation signal worsened through expectations and survey-price channels. S&P PMI delivered the week’s business-side stagflationary mix: modest output, weak demand quality and rising price pressure. UMich then turned the household side policy-relevant, with 1-year inflation expectations rising to 4.8% and 5-year expectations to 3.9%. The week did not deliver clean disinflation cover.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Mortgage transmission re-tightened immediately after the early-week housing stabilization signals, with the MBA 30-year rate rising to 6.56%. That kept housing in a binding affordability regime and weakened the single-family channel despite aggregate construction resilience. UMich also showed households registering the shock as a cost-of-living squeeze, especially through gasoline and inflation expectations.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The policy constraint tightened materially during the week. The data tape already made a clean easing signal difficult: claims stayed low, PMI / regional survey prices remained elevated, mortgage transmission re-tightened, and UMich 5-year inflation expectations rose to 3.9%. The Fed tape then institutionalized that constraint: the April FOMC minutes showed a majority willing to consider firming if inflation persisted, Waller explicitly removed the clean-cut path by saying hikes could not be ruled out, and Warsh inherited a divided but increasingly hawkish committee. The cut bar is now inflation improvement or a clear labor deterioration, not weak sentiment alone.

C. FULL RELEASE ARCHIVE

MONDAY 18 MAY 2026

United States — NAHB Housing Market Index (May)

Release Date: 18 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

NAHB Housing Market Index: 37 | Prior: 34 | Poll: 34 | Surprise: +3.0

Professional Market Commentary

The professional tape framed the print as a modest bounce from a depressed base, not a housing recovery. The scout’s first-pass read emphasized that all three NAHB components improved, but that the level remained structurally weak, with the headline still below the 50 breakeven and builder incentives still elevated.

Official NAHB framing supports that interpretation: builder confidence improved, but affordability remained the binding constraint. The tape highlighted elevated mortgage rates, rising energy costs, Iran-related uncertainty, sticky incentives, and persistent sub-50 readings across all major subcomponents.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a false-dawn housing print rather than a clean improvement signal.

The headline beat matters because it confirms that buyer demand is not collapsing at current mortgage-rate levels. The breadth also matters: current sales, sales expectations and buyer traffic all rose. That is a better internal structure than a one-component rebound. But the level is still weak: all three components remain below 50, buyer traffic remains extremely depressed, and incentives remain sticky. The price-cut share fell, but the average reduction widened, which means fewer builders are cutting, but those cutting are cutting more deeply.

The macro signal is therefore not “housing recovery.” It is “demand floor visible, rate and affordability ceiling unchanged.” The print offsets the April deterioration but does not erase it.

State transition: false dawn.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD housing sequence had already deteriorated through weak builder sentiment, falling permits, soft home-price breadth and rate-sensitive turnover. May HMI partially offsets the April builder-sentiment drop, but the incentive and traffic details keep the sector inside a constrained housing regime. Housing remains a rate-sensitive drag rather than a new growth engine.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This print marginally weakens the housing-collapse story but does not change the Fed reaction function.

The strategist read is conditional: if mortgage rates stabilize or fall, the housing complex has enough pent-up demand to stabilize further; if long rates remain pressured by oil, inflation risk or term premium, the improvement should fade quickly. For rates, the print is not dovish enough to force easing and not strong enough to validate a cyclical housing re-rating. It keeps the Fed in a hold-biased, data-dependent regime: housing softness is visible, but not yet severe enough to dominate inflation and labor constraints.

TUESDAY 19 MAY 2026

United States — Redbook YY (w/e 11 May)

Release Date: 19 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:55
Importance: *

Reuters Data

Redbook YY: 8.1% | Prior: 9.6%

Professional Market Commentary

No Tier 1 institutional debate detected.

The scout tape treats Redbook as a low-tier but useful high-frequency nominal spending cross-check. The print decelerated from 9.6% to 8.1%, but remained in an elevated YoY range and therefore does not indicate a clean consumer break.

Comment — Economist Layer

Redbook confirms nominal consumption resilience, not real-demand acceleration.

The useful signal is the level: +8.1% y/y remains elevated enough to push back against a near-term consumer collapse narrative. The weekly deceleration from +9.6% is not sufficient to call a turn, especially because the series is nominal, high-frequency and not compositionally rich. Against the broader USD regime, the print preserves the same split: households are still transacting, but the real signal is clouded by inflation, gasoline and price-level effects.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive already showed Redbook as a nominal-spending resilience proxy that offsets weak sentiment without proving real consumption strength. This print preserves that interpretation: nominal activity is still elevated, but the real-consumption quality signal remains unresolved. The consumer bucket remains employment-supported and inflation-constrained.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Low standalone policy relevance.

For the Fed, Redbook does not decide anything. It keeps the consumer-resilience story alive at the margin, but it is too nominal and too noisy to override official retail sales, income, real consumption or labor data. The useful cross-asset implication is only that weak sentiment should not be mechanically translated into an immediate spending break while high-frequency nominal spending remains elevated.

United States — Pending Homes Index / Pending Sales Change MM (Apr)

Release Date: 19 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Pending Homes Index: 74.8 | Prior: 73.7 | Revised: 73.8
Pending Sales Change MM: 1.4% | Prior: 1.5% | Revised: 1.7% | Poll: 1.0% | Surprise: +0.40%

Professional Market Commentary

The scout tape frames the NAR release as a conditional stabilization signal: April pending sales beat expectations, the prior month was revised higher, and the regional pattern showed three of four regions improving month-on-month. The tape also stresses that the recovery remains rate-capped and supply-constrained rather than self-sustaining.

Official and trade-press framing in the scout was consistent: buyers were described as cautiously returning, but the rate ceiling, affordability constraint and supply shortage remained central to the interpretation.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the strongest constructive housing signal in the 18–19 May tape, but still conditional.

The primary signal is persistence. March pending sales were revised up to +1.7% m/m, and April added another +1.4% m/m gain. That makes the release more meaningful than a one-month bounce. The index level also improved to 74.8 from a revised 73.8. Regionally, the scout highlighted Northeast and Midwest strength, modest West improvement and South softness, which points to broader but still uneven participation.

The hierarchy is clear: contract activity is stabilizing before closed-sales data, but the rate and affordability ceiling remains binding. The fact that professional commentary kept mortgage rates and supply constraints central prevents a clean “housing upturn” label. The correct read is demand floor, not demand breakout.

State transition: stabilization.

Context from prior regional live file: March pending home sales had already been treated as a false dawn because the improvement came from a weak base and remained dependent on mortgage-rate relief. April is the second consecutive positive print and therefore upgrades the signal from one-off bounce to conditional stabilization. The housing bucket is no longer simply deteriorating, but it remains capped by financing costs and affordability.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This matters for the reaction function only at the margin.

The print reduces urgency for a housing-led easing argument because turnover is proving more resilient than expected. But it does not create a hawkish-growth impulse either, because the level remains depressed and the recovery is conditional on mortgage rates not backing up. For rates and USD, the implication is asymmetry: housing is no longer an immediate downside shock, but it remains a vulnerable transmission channel if inflation or oil pressure keeps long rates elevated.

WEDNESDAY 20 MAY 2026

United States — MBA 30-Year Mortgage Rate (w/e 11 May)

Release Date: 20 May 2026 | Local Time: 13:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

MBA 30-Year Mortgage Rate: 6.56% | Prior: 6.46%

Professional Market Commentary

The MBA block confirms that the housing-rate ceiling re-tightened immediately after the NAHB and pending-sales stabilization signals. The scout tape reported the 30-year conforming contract rate rising to 6.56% from 6.46%, with total mortgage applications down, purchase applications lower, and refinance applications essentially flat.

The scout tape’s framing is therefore coherent with the official MBA signal: demand has not collapsed year-on-year, but the weekly purchase pullback shows the marginal buyer remains highly rate-sensitive.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the financing-cost confirmation of the early-week housing constraint.

The level matters more than the weekly move. A 6.56% MBA conforming mortgage rate keeps the housing market inside a binding affordability regime. The purchase-index decline shows the rate channel is biting at the margin, even if year-on-year demand remains positive. This resolves the tension between NAHB / NAR stabilization and the broader housing constraint: demand exists, but the financing channel caps conversion.

The hierarchy is therefore clear: NAHB and pending sales show a demand floor; MBA shows the ceiling tightening. That means housing is stabilizing only conditionally, not entering a self-sustaining recovery.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive already treated the mid-6% mortgage-rate environment as the main housing transmission channel. This print extends that pressure by moving the rate higher again after the prior week’s 6.46% level. Housing remains rate-capped even where demand indicators have improved.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not a standalone Fed event, but it matters as a transmission-channel check.

The print supports a tighter-financial-conditions read for rate-sensitive sectors. For Fed pricing, it cuts both ways: higher mortgage rates weaken housing activity over time, but if the rate move is driven by inflation, energy or term-premium pressure, the central bank does not receive a clean easing signal. The strategic trigger is mortgage-rate persistence. A move back toward 6.20% would validate housing stabilization; a sustained mid-6% or higher rate keeps single-family activity vulnerable.

THURSDAY 21 MAY 2026

United States — Building Permits / Housing Starts (Apr)

Release Date: 21 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Building Permits: 1.442M | Prior: 1.363M | Poll: 1.385M | Surprise: +0.0570M
Building Permits Change MM: 5.8% | Prior: -11.4% | Revised: -11.5%
Housing Starts: 1.465M | Prior: 1.502M | Revised: 1.507M | Poll: 1.410M | Surprise: +0.0550M
Housing Starts Change MM: -2.8% | Prior: 10.8% | Revised: 12.0%

Professional Market Commentary

The headline was better than expected, but the professional tape treated the release as compositionally weak. The scout tape emphasized that the permits and starts headline beats masked single-family softness, while multi-family strength carried the aggregate.

The composition is the core signal: the April release confirms that aggregate residential construction can look resilient even while the owner-occupier, mortgage-sensitive single-family channel is weakening.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a headline beat with weak underlying housing quality.

The headline permits beat and the headline starts beat would normally support a constructive read. But the composition reverses the signal. Single-family permits are the cleaner forward indicator for owner-occupier supply, and they fell for a second consecutive month. Single-family starts then confirmed the pressure with a 9.0% decline. Multi-family strength improves the aggregate level, but it is less directly tied to the mortgage-sensitive household demand channel.

The correct hierarchy is therefore: aggregate construction looks resilient, but the single-family channel is deteriorating. That matters because the early-week NAHB and NAR prints showed demand floor formation, while MBA showed the mortgage ceiling tightening. This release confirms the same bifurcation: housing is not collapsing in aggregate, but the rate-sensitive single-family impulse is weakening.

State transition: false dawn.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD housing archive already showed suppressed activity, sticky affordability pressure and restrictive mortgage rates. April permits and starts do not overturn that regime because the headline beat is carried by multi-family rather than single-family. The housing bucket remains a constrained stabilization story, not a clean improvement story.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The market should not trade this as a clean housing beat.

For the Fed, the release does not provide a strong growth-positive signal because the most policy-sensitive housing channel — single-family construction — is weakening. But it also does not deliver an outright recession signal because aggregate construction is being buffered by multi-family. The rates implication is conditional: if mortgage rates fall, single-family permits can stabilize; if mortgage rates remain elevated, the permits decline should feed into softer starts later.

United States — Initial Jobless Claims / 4-Week Average / Continuing Claims

Release Date: 21 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Initial Jobless Claims: 209k | Prior: 212k | Poll: 210k | Surprise: -1.0k
Jobless Claims 4-Week Average: 202.50k | Prior: 204.00k
Continuing Jobless Claims: 1.782M | Prior: 1.776M | Poll: 1.785M | Surprise: -0.0030M

Professional Market Commentary

The DOL claims block was the labor anchor of the week. The scout tape framed the release as low-friction labor-market continuation: initial claims were slightly better than consensus, the four-week average declined, continuing claims were little changed, and the insured unemployment rate remained stable.

Professional framing was straightforward: layoffs remain contained, and the claims channel does not yet validate a recessionary labor break.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the key labor anchor of the week.

Claims are not deteriorating. Initial claims are essentially in-line to slightly better than consensus, the four-week average is falling, continuing claims are stable below 1.8M, and the insured unemployment rate is unchanged. The labor-market signal is therefore “low-fire,” even if other data suggest softer hiring or weaker business confidence.

The distinction matters. PMI employment softness and regional manufacturing weakness can imply slower hiring, hours pressure or caution, but claims measure separations. As long as claims remain near 200k and continuing claims stay contained, the Fed does not have a labor-side reason to prioritize downside growth risk over inflation risk.

State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD sequence already showed labor as firm enough to block an easing trigger, with claims low and broader employment composites stabilizing. This release extends that pattern. Labor remains the main macro anchor preventing the stagflationary soft-data deterioration from becoming a recession call.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is hawkish by omission.

The release does not create a fresh tightening impulse, but it removes the labor-market justification for a dovish reaction to weak surveys. For the Fed, the reaction-function implication is clear: if inflation pressure and survey weakness coexist with claims still low, policy remains constrained. The key break line remains a sustained move in initial claims above roughly 230k or continuing claims clearly above the recent range.

United States — Philly Fed Business Index (May)

Release Date: 21 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Philly Fed Business Index: -0.4 | Prior: 26.7 | Poll: 18.0 | Surprise: -18.40

Professional Market Commentary

The headline miss was severe: the Philadelphia Fed index fell to -0.4 from 26.7, versus expectations near 18. Professional coverage emphasized a sharp loss of current momentum, while also noting that future activity improved, preventing a simple collapse interpretation. The scout tape adds the critical internal detail: new orders and shipments fell sharply, prices paid and received declined but remained elevated, employment stayed negative, and the future activity index rose strongly.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a deterioration print, but not a clean recession print.

The headline move is large enough to matter. A fall from 26.7 to -0.4 turns a strong expansion reading into a near-stall / mild contraction signal in one month. New orders moving negative is the important demand component. Shipments staying positive and future activity rising prevent the release from being read as a full collapse.

The better interpretation is front-loading payback plus stagflationary pressure. April’s strength likely overstated underlying momentum, and May corrects that excess. But prices still elevated and employment still negative mean the internal mix remains policy-unfriendly: weaker activity without clean disinflation or a labor-market break.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had just recorded a very strong Empire State survey with high prices and strong orders, reinforcing nominal activity resilience. Philly Fed now offsets that regional strength and introduces dispersion across manufacturing surveys. The manufacturing bucket shifts from broad resilience to uneven, price-constrained softness.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The headline is dovish for growth, but the composition is not enough to force a Fed pivot.

A single regional Fed miss can move front-end sentiment intraday, but it needs confirmation from national ISM, hard orders, production or claims. The strategic read is conditional: if June Philly remains sub-zero and ISM confirms weaker orders, this becomes a broader manufacturing deterioration signal; if Philly rebounds, May looks like a front-loading payback artifact. For now, it raises growth-downside risk without lowering the inflation constraint enough to unlock cuts.

United States — S&P Global Manufacturing PMI Flash / Services PMI Flash / Composite PMI Flash (May)

Release Date: 21 May 2026 | Local Time: 15:45
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

S&P Global Manufacturing PMI Flash: 55.3 | Prior: 54.5 | Poll: 53.8 | Surprise: +1.50
S&P Global Services PMI Flash: 50.9 | Prior: 51.0 | Poll: 51.1 | Surprise: -0.20
S&P Global Composite PMI Flash: 51.7 | Prior: 51.7

Professional Market Commentary

The headline split is deceptive. The scout tape emphasized that composite output was unchanged, services were barely expansionary, and manufacturing strength was flattered by precautionary stockbuilding / supplier-delivery distortions rather than clean final-demand acceleration.

The institutional read is therefore not “manufacturing boom.” It is subdued growth, rising prices, weak demand quality and an adverse employment signal.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is one of the most important releases of the week because it crystallizes the stagflationary signal.

A naive reading would treat manufacturing at 55.3 as a strong cyclical positive. The release does not allow that interpretation. Manufacturing strength is inflated by precautionary inventories and supplier-delivery distortions. Services at 50.9 are barely expansionary. Composite output at 51.7 is not accelerating. Order-book commentary and employment weakness point to weak demand quality under rising cost pressure.

The signal hierarchy is therefore adverse: prices up, growth modest, services soft, manufacturing flattered by supply-side mechanics. This is not recession, because the composite remains above 50 and claims remain low. But it is not healthy expansion either. It is a supply-shock / cost-push growth squeeze.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had already moved into stagflation confirmation after CPI, PPI, import prices and Empire State prices broadened the inflation signal while hard activity stayed resilient. The flash PMI extends that regime into current business surveys. It shifts the week from housing false dawn toward broader stagflationary bifurcation: labor is holding, but business growth quality is weakening under price pressure.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is a major release for the Fed reaction-function map.

It creates the uncomfortable configuration: activity soft enough to raise growth concern, prices hot enough to block a clean easing signal, and claims still low enough to deny the Fed a labor-side pivot. For rates, the print is two-sided but ultimately policy-constraining. A pure growth slowdown would be dovish; a price-surge slowdown is not. The next decisive test is whether ISM Manufacturing confirms weak orders and high prices, or whether S&P is overstating the supply-chain distortion.

United States — KC Fed Manufacturing / Composite Index (May)

Release Date: 21 May 2026 | Local Time: 17:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

KC Fed Manufacturing: 9 | Prior: 10
KC Fed Composite Index: 8 | Prior: 10

Professional Market Commentary

KC Fed showed continued expansion but mild deceleration. The month-over-month composite index fell to 8 from 10, while the year-over-year composite rose and future activity edged up. KC Fed also noted durable-manufacturing slowing, especially transportation equipment, while nondurable manufacturing continued to improve.

The scout tape emphasizes the cross-regional contrast: Philly cratered, while KC remained positive. That makes the manufacturing signal dispersed rather than uniformly collapsing.

Comment — Economist Layer

KC Fed is a stabilizing cross-check against the Philly shock, but not a clean positive.

The MoM composite is still expansionary, but it is decelerating. The YoY improvement looks better, but base effects and regional mix matter. The internal message is mixed: durable activity is losing momentum, transportation equipment is weak, and raw-material price pressure remains elevated, but the broader Tenth District is not following Philly into contraction.

The key analytical value is dispersion. The manufacturing complex is not uniformly breaking; it is fragmenting by region, sector and exposure to supply-chain / tariff / energy channels. That fits the S&P PMI message: manufacturing headline strength cannot be interpreted as clean demand strength.

State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had shown manufacturing resilience through Empire State and industrial production, but with price pressure already elevated. KC Fed preserves the resilience leg, while Philly and S&P weaken the quality of that resilience. Manufacturing remains above recession threshold in places, but the regime is more uneven and cost-constrained.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

KC Fed prevents over-trading the Philly collapse as a national manufacturing break.

For the Fed, the release is not dovish enough to matter alone. It keeps the manufacturing story conditional: if ISM confirms Philly weakness, regional softness becomes national; if ISM holds and KC remains positive, the Fed will view the weakness as dispersion rather than broad labor-market-relevant deterioration. The strategic signal is therefore “monitor dispersion,” not “trade recession.”

FRIDAY 22 MAY 2026

United States — Leading Index Change MM (Apr)

Release Date: 22 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Leading Index Change MM: 0.1% | Prior: -0.6% | Poll: -0.2% | Surprise: +0.30%

Professional Market Commentary

The Conference Board LEI rose 0.1% in April to 97.4 after March’s 0.6% decline. The six-month change remained negative at -0.7%, though less severe than the prior six-month contraction. The Conference Board’s own framing was cautious: April’s bounce did not offset the March fall, and the six- and twelve-month growth rates still signaled fragile conditions ahead.

The scout tape adds the relevant composition: the positive contribution came mainly from stock prices and building permits, specifically the multi-family / two-plus-unit channel, which is consistent with the housing-starts composition from the prior day.

Comment — Economist Layer

LEI is a small positive headline but not a macro reset.

The release matters because it confirms the same bifurcation seen across the week. The index bounced, but the trend remains negative. The improvement was helped by market prices and multi-family permits, while the consumer side remains fragile. That means the LEI does not contradict the stagflationary week signal; it softens the recession probability mechanically but leaves the forward growth-quality problem intact.

The hierarchy is: CEI still firm, LEI trend still weak, consumer-side pressure rising, structural capex / market-price offsets cushioning the aggregate. This is not a deterioration print, but it is also not enough to upgrade the regional state.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD sequence had already shown forward-looking softness alongside resilient hard activity. April LEI preserves that split: the coincident economy has not broken, but leading conditions remain fragile. The release confirms bifurcation rather than changing the regime.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Low immediate market relevance, useful as a confirmation layer.

The print reduces the risk of over-interpreting every soft survey as imminent recession, because the LEI did not extend March’s decline. But it does not offset the more market-relevant UMich / PMI inflation-expectations problem. For the Fed, LEI is not the driver; it only confirms that the growth slowdown is uneven, with enough structural and market support to prevent a clean downside-growth policy pivot.

United States — University of Michigan Sentiment Final / Conditions Final / Expectations Final / Inflation Expectations (May)

Release Date: 22 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

U Mich Sentiment Final: 44.8 | Prior: 48.2 | Poll: 48.2 | Surprise: -3.40
U Mich Conditions Final: 45.8 | Prior: 47.8 | Poll: 47.9 | Surprise: -2.10
U Mich Expectations Final: 44.1 | Prior: 48.5 | Poll: 48.4 | Surprise: -4.30
U Mich 1Yr Inflation Final: 4.8% | Prior: 4.5%
U Mich 5-Yr Inflation Final: 3.9% | Prior: 3.4%

Professional Market Commentary

This is the decisive Friday release. University of Michigan final data show sentiment at 44.8, current conditions at 45.8 and expectations at 44.1 in May. The survey also shows year-ahead inflation expectations at 4.8% and long-run expectations at 3.9%, notably above the 2024 range and higher than April.

Reuters framed the print as a record-low sentiment shock driven by rising cost-of-living pressure, especially gasoline prices linked to the Iran war, with inflation expectations increasing. The professional market relevance is not just the sentiment collapse; it is the long-run inflation-expectations move, because that is the part the Fed cannot comfortably look through.

Fed Governor Waller’s same-day speech sharpened the policy link: he said some one-to-five-year expectations had moved up since the start of 2026 and that he found this concerning, while Reuters separately reported his stance as removing the easing bias and warning that rate-cut talk was inappropriate in the current inflation configuration.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the strongest deterioration signal of the week.

The headline consumer signal is already severe: final sentiment was revised down sharply from the preliminary estimate, current conditions fell, and expectations fell even more. But the macro hierarchy is not simply “consumer confidence weak.” The dominant signal is the combination of consumer capitulation with higher inflation expectations. One-year expectations at 4.8% are bad; five-year expectations at 3.9% are the policy problem.

That composition changes the interpretation of the whole week. Earlier data showed housing false dawn, manufacturing-price pressure, weak survey orders, and labor resilience. UMich closes the loop: households are now registering the supply shock as both a present-income squeeze and a longer-run inflation problem. This is the worst configuration for policy because the data give both camps ammunition: the growth side can point to collapsing sentiment; the hawkish side can point to inflation-expectations drift.

The demographic and political breadth also matters. The scout tape flags particular weakness among lower-income / non-college groups and a sharper deterioration among Republicans and independents, while Democrats were less changed. That implies the shock is not only partisan-noise rotation; it is a cost-of-living and gasoline-price transmission hitting credit- and cash-flow-sensitive households.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had already treated sentiment weakness as a major household-side warning, but labor and spending resilience kept it from becoming a decisive macro break. May final UMich changes that balance because inflation expectations deteriorate alongside the sentiment collapse. The consumer bucket shifts from weak-sentiment / resilient-spending tension toward consumer capitulation with inflation-expectations risk.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the week’s top Fed-reaction-function release.

The immediate policy read is not straightforwardly dovish. In a normal cycle, record-low sentiment and weaker expectations would argue for downside-growth risk and lower rates. In this configuration, the five-year inflation-expectations move blocks that read. The Fed can acknowledge consumer stress, but it cannot easily ease into rising medium- and long-run inflation expectations while claims remain low and price components in PMI / regional Fed surveys are still elevated.

Rates implication: bear-flattening / front-end hawkish risk remains alive if officials treat expectations drift as the dominant signal; growth-dovish steepening risk reappears only if hard consumption and claims confirm the sentiment collapse. FX implication: USD support can persist through policy-constraint channels, but the quality of support deteriorates if U.S. growth data start confirming the consumer capitulation. The decisive next test is June preliminary UMich, especially whether 5-year expectations fall back below 3.5% or move toward / above 4.0%.

CENTRAL BANK TAPE

Summary — Fed reaction function

The Fed tape turns the week from a pure data-driven stagflationary signal into a policy-regime inflection. The data already showed the problem: consumer capitulation, weak demand quality, rising inflation expectations, sticky survey prices and stable claims. The central-bank tape supplies the reaction-function translation: the Fed is no longer operating with a credible “next move = cut” bias. The reaction function has bifurcated into hold-or-hike, with cuts requiring either visible inflation improvement or a clear labor-market break.

The dominant policy constraint is now expectations risk. The May UMich 5-year inflation-expectations print gives the Fed a concrete reason not to ease into consumer weakness, while claims near 200k deny the Fed a labor-side pivot. The week therefore marks a shift from “growth weakness may eventually force cuts” to “growth weakness is insufficient unless inflation expectations re-anchor or labor deteriorates.”

FOMC Minutes — April 28–29 meeting, released 20 May 2026
Importance: ****

Key policy signal: The minutes confirmed that the hawkish shift was institutional, not limited to the three dissenters. The meeting decision held the fed funds range at 3.50%–3.75%, but the vote was 8–4, described in the scout as the most dissented decision since 1992. Hammack, Logan and Kashkari dissented against the easing-bias language, while Miran dissented in favor of a cut. More importantly, the minutes indicated that a majority saw policy firming as potentially appropriate if inflation remained persistently above 2%, and many preferred removing the easing-bias language from the statement.

Representative quote: “some policy firming would likely become appropriate if inflation were to continue to run persistently above 2 percent.”

Incremental reaction function: This breaks the previous “hold now, cut later” baseline. The Fed is no longer just delaying cuts; it is reopening the possibility that the next policy move could be a hike if energy / input-cost pass-through keeps inflation elevated. The Middle East / crude-oil channel is explicitly central to this shift, because the minutes flagged the higher crude futures curve and the risk that higher energy and input costs could delay the return to 2%.

Governor Christopher Waller — “The Economic Outlook,” Frankfurt, 22 May 2026
Importance: ****

Key policy signal: Waller is the decisive speaker because he had previously been part of the more dovish wing. His pivot converts the hawkish minutes into an operational reaction-function signal. The scout notes that Waller said he could no longer rule out rate hikes if inflation failed to abate, explicitly linked policy risk to rising one-to-five-year inflation expectations, called for removing the easing-bias language, and set a high bar for cuts.

Representative quotes: “I can no longer rule out rate hikes further down the road if inflation does not abate soon.”
“If I believe inflation expectations start to become unanchored, I would not hesitate to support an increase.”
“I am going to need to see improvement on inflation or a significant deterioration in the labor market.”

Incremental reaction function: This is the week’s cleanest policy translation of the UMich shock. Waller’s framework says weak sentiment is not enough; the Fed needs either inflation improvement or a labor break. Since claims are still contained and inflation expectations have moved higher, the dovish path is blocked. His pivot also matters because it comes from a conditional voter, not from the already-visible hawkish dissenters. That makes the reaction-function shift more durable.

Chair Kevin Warsh — swearing-in remarks, 22 May 2026
Importance: ***

Key policy signal: Warsh’s remarks were brief and ceremonial, but structurally important because they mark the institutional handover. His “reform-oriented Federal Reserve” language suggests a shift in framework, communication and possibly balance-sheet doctrine, while the immediate policy path is constrained by the hawkish committee he inherits. The scout frames the event as less important for the speech text itself than for the political-doctrinal tension: Trump appointed Warsh amid pressure for cuts, but Warsh inherits a committee whose minutes and Waller speech point toward hold-or-hike optionality.

Representative quote: “I will lead a reform-oriented Federal Reserve.”

Incremental reaction function: Warsh himself did not deliver a new rate-path signal. The policy shift came from the minutes and Waller. But Warsh’s arrival changes the institutional wrapper: the new Chair starts with a divided committee, a hawkish majority signal, inflation-expectations pressure, and political pressure for cuts. His first operational test is whether he aligns with the hawkish committee, tries to rebuild a unified hold path, or uses “reform” language to separate Fed doctrine from both Powell-era communication and Trump’s rate-cut preference.

Bottom line — central bank tape

The Fed-speak tape confirms that the week’s data deterioration does not create a clean dovish pivot. The data say stagflationary deterioration; the Fed tape says the reaction function has adjusted in real time. The new policy regime is: hold as base case, hike as live tail, cut only if inflation expectations re-anchor or labor breaks. The main falsifier is still Hormuz / energy de-escalation, because it would simultaneously ease the inflation-expectations shock and give conditional hawks like Waller a path back toward cuts.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Labor remains stable and some headline activity indicators are still above breakeven, but the consumer / survey / price-expectations complex has deteriorated sharply.
Why it matters: This is not a clean recession tape because claims remain low; it is not a clean resilience tape because UMich inflation expectations, PMI prices, mortgage rates and housing composition all point to a worsening policy constraint.
Implication: The USD regime is policy-constrained stagflationary deterioration, conditional on whether the energy / long-rate / inflation-expectations shock reverses or propagates into hard consumption and labor data.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

University of Michigan Sentiment Final / Inflation Expectations (22 May)
Why it matters: This is the most decision-relevant release of the week. Sentiment capitulated, but the critical policy signal is the 5-year inflation-expectations rise to 3.9%, which turns consumer weakness into a Fed constraint rather than a clean dovish growth shock.
S&P Global Manufacturing PMI Flash / Services PMI Flash / Composite PMI Flash (21 May)
Why it matters: The PMI complex gives the cleanest business-side stagflationary read: modest composite growth, barely expansionary services, manufacturing strength distorted by supply mechanics, and rising price pressure.
Initial Jobless Claims / Continuing Claims (21 May)
Why it matters: Claims are the labor anchor that prevents the week from becoming a recession call. As long as initial claims stay near 200k and continuing claims remain contained, the Fed has limited labor-side justification to look through inflation-expectations drift.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 11 May 2026 to 15 May 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The US week closed as a synchronized stagflation-confirmation tape: CPI delivered the household-price shock, PPI validated the wholesale / margin pipeline, import prices confirmed the external channel, and Empire State prices showed the same pressure at regional manufacturing level. Growth did not break: retail control held, business inventories were sales-supported, Empire State orders surged, and industrial production rebounded with upward revisions. Labor also stayed too firm for cuts, with ETI stabilization and claims still low despite a marginal weekly rise.

The resulting regime is not clean overheating and not recession: nominal activity remains resilient, but real quality is weakening through negative real earnings, price-supported retail flow, fragile consumer sentiment, restrictive mortgage rates, and sticky housing prices. Policy tension therefore moved toward hawkish hold / tightening-communication risk: inflation broadened across channels, while labor and hard activity failed to provide the Fed a credible easing trigger.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: +
Supporting evidence:
Retail sales rose 0.5% m/m and control group rose 0.5%, blocking an immediate consumer-break narrative despite weaker real-income quality. Empire State jumped to 19.6 with new orders at 22.7, while industrial production rose 0.7%, manufacturing output rose 0.6%, and March IP was revised less negative. Business inventories rose strongly, but sales rose faster and the inventory/sales ratio fell, preventing an involuntary-overhang signal.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Employment Trends rose to 105.77 and internal breadth stabilized, supporting “layoffs not yet breaking.” Claims softened marginally to 211k, but the four-week average remained low at 203.75k and continuing claims were below consensus. Labor is cooling at the margin but still too firm to validate cuts.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
CPI accelerated to 3.8% y/y and core CPI re-accelerated to 0.4% m/m / 2.8% y/y. PPI then broadened the shock, with final demand up 1.4% m/m / 6.0% y/y and ex food, energy and trade up 0.6% m/m / 4.4% y/y. Import prices rose 1.9% m/m and 4.2% y/y, while Empire State prices paid and received moved to their highest levels since 2022. The inflation signal clearly deteriorated across consumer, producer, border-price and survey channels.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
The MBA 30-year mortgage rate stayed restrictive at 6.46%, keeping the housing-affordability channel active. Import-price and PPI shocks raise input-cost and margin pressure, while hot inflation plus resilient activity supports front-end policy rigidity and USD resilience. Financial conditions remain restrictive for rate-sensitive sectors but not restrictive enough to break aggregate demand.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The Fed’s easing case weakened because inflation broadened while claims, retail sales, Empire State and industrial production all rejected a clean growth-break offset. Capacity utilization below long-run norms limits a pure hike narrative, but the week shifts the reaction function toward hawkish hold and possible tightening communication rather than cuts.

C. FULL RELEASE ARCHIVE

MONDAY 11 MAY 2026

United States — Employment Trends Index (Apr)
Release Date: 11 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Employment Trends: 105.77 | Prior: 105.72 | Revised: 105.52

Professional Market Commentary

The Conference Board framed the April ETI as a modest labor-resilience signal. The index rose to 105.77 from a downwardly revised 105.52, with the official release emphasizing that the recent stability was a break from the ETI’s downward drift through 2025. The constructive internal details were lower “jobs hard to get,” more small firms unable to fill positions, lower claims, and temporary-help employment adding positively; the sole negative was a higher involuntary part-time-worker share.

The professional read is therefore not a labor-break signal. It is a leading-index stabilization print after a softer prior trend, but the downward revision to March reduces the strength of the level narrative.

Comment — Economist Layer

The ETI improves the labor-stability leg, but the signal is not clean enough to offset the broader stagflationary mix. The index is up on the month, breadth is positive, and several forward-looking employment inputs improved. However, the March downward revision matters: what looked like a stable prior month is now a softer base, so the April increase is more of a rebound from revised weakness than a clean new acceleration.

The internal hierarchy is important. Lower “jobs hard to get” and higher unfilled small-firm positions point to labor-market tightness still present at the margin, while the involuntary part-time-worker deterioration introduces an underemployment quality caveat. The release therefore supports “layoffs not yet breaking” rather than “labor demand re-accelerating.”

State transition: stabilization.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD sequence already showed labor too firm to validate cuts, with claims low and compensation still restrictive. ETI extends that resilience but does not remove the deterioration risk embedded in weaker survey confidence and softer employment-quality indicators. The labor bucket remains policy-constraining rather than recession-confirming.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not a Fed pivot release. It marginally supports the hawkish-hold side of the reaction function because the leading employment composite is stabilizing and the claims input remains benign.

The strategic use is as a labor-side cross-check ahead of jobless claims. If claims remain low later in the week, ETI will look like confirmation that the Fed cannot lean on labor weakness to cut. If claims deteriorate, ETI becomes a lagged composite rebound rather than a fresh signal.

United States — Existing Home Sales / Existing Home Sales % Change (Apr)
Release Date: 11 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Existing Home Sales: 4.02M | Prior: 3.98M | Revised: 4.01M | Reuters Poll: 4.05M | Surprise: -0.030M
Exist. Home Sales % Chg: 0.2% | Prior: -3.6% | Revised: -2.9%

Professional Market Commentary

The NAR release framed April as a marginal stabilization print rather than a recovery. Sales rose 0.2% m/m to a 4.02M SAAR, missed consensus, and were flat y/y. Inventory rose 5.8% m/m to 1.47M units, equal to 4.4 months’ supply, while the median price rose 0.9% y/y to $417,700, the 34th consecutive y/y price increase. NAR also highlighted improved affordability, but Chief Economist Lawrence Yun caveated that inventory remains tight and that consumers are taking more time before transacting.

The tape read is “sluggish but not collapsing.” Inventory is rebuilding, days on market are lengthening y/y, and regional performance is uneven, with the South and Midwest positive m/m, the Northeast flat, and the West weaker.

Comment — Economist Layer

The housing signal is stabilization at a depressed level, not a clean restart. The headline level missed consensus despite the small m/m gain, and the positive March revision prevents a worse sequential story. The more useful signal is the mix: inventory is improving, but not enough to break the lock-in / affordability constraint; prices remain sticky despite weak turnover; and regional dispersion shows that the recovery is not broad.

This is a stagflationary housing configuration. Activity is subdued because rates and sentiment still bind, but prices are not falling fast enough to generate a quick shelter-disinflation impulse. The rise in inventory and y/y lengthening of days on market are medium-term cooling inputs, but the still-positive price trend keeps the near-term inflation relief limited.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had already treated housing as a rate-sensitive downside channel, with weak resale activity, affordability pressure, and price breadth deterioration. April existing-home sales keep that housing bucket suppressed but stable. The release does not shift the regime; it preserves housing as a growth drag rather than a fast disinflation catalyst.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The release has low standalone Fed impact but matters for the growth-quality side of the tape. It supports neither a clean recession trade nor a clean housing recovery trade.

For rates, the housing channel remains a slow-burn tightening transmission mechanism. For the Fed, weak transactions help the growth-downside argument, but sticky prices and only gradual inventory normalization do not solve the inflation problem. The next housing-relevant check is whether mortgage rates and applications confirm that April’s stabilization can persist.

TUESDAY 12 MAY 2026

United States — NFIB Business Optimism Index (Apr)
Release Date: 12 May 2026 | Local Time: 12:00
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

NFIB Business Optimism Idx: 95.9 | Prior: 95.8

Professional Market Commentary

The official NFIB framing was headline stability with weak underlying quality. The optimism index rose only 0.1 point to 95.9, remaining below its 52-year average of 98.0 for a second month, while the Uncertainty Index fell to 88 but stayed far above its historical average. NFIB’s release emphasized labor quality as the top issue, a softer Employment Index, and persistent pricing pressure.

The institutional tape is thin because NFIB rarely receives standalone macro-desk treatment. The useful market interpretation is internal: small-business pricing pressure is rising while employment and expansion signals are not improving enough to validate a demand-led growth story.

Comment — Economist Layer

NFIB is more important than the flat headline suggests. A 0.1-point rise in the optimism index is not the macro signal. The signal is the combination of persistent price-setting pressure, still-elevated uncertainty, labor-quality constraints, and softer employment momentum.

This is a Main Street stagflation print. Small businesses are not reporting a clean demand boom, but they are still passing through costs and remain cautious about expansion. That matters because it links the CPI shock to the business-pricing channel: if firms are still raising selling prices while confidence and employment components soften, the economy is absorbing a supply-cost shock rather than enjoying healthy nominal acceleration.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD sequence had already moved into stagflation-confirmation, with resilient nominal activity, weak real-quality indicators, and rising price pressure across PCE, GDP prices, ISM prices and Fed communication. NFIB extends that pattern into the small-business channel. It reinforces the inflation constraint without upgrading growth quality.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

NFIB is not a direct market mover, but it is a useful reaction-function cross-check. It weakens the argument that price pressure is only a large-firm, energy or survey artifact.

For the Fed, the implication is hold-biased. Elevated price plans and fragile confidence are exactly the mix that keeps policy constrained: too much inflation pressure to cut, not enough clean growth strength to become outright hawkish-growth. Claims and retail sales are the next tests for whether Main Street caution is translating into realized labor or demand weakness.

United States — CPI / Core CPI / CPI-W (Apr)
Release Date: 12 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

CPI MM, SA: 0.6% | Prior: 0.9% | Reuters Poll: 0.6% | Surprise: 0.00%
CPI YY, NSA: 3.8% | Prior: 3.3% | Reuters Poll: 3.7% | Surprise: 0.10%
Core CPI MM, SA: 0.4% | Prior: 0.2% | Reuters Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: 0.10%
Core CPI YY, NSA: 2.8% | Prior: 2.6% | Reuters Poll: 2.7% | Surprise: 0.10%
CPI Index, NSA: 333.020 | Prior: 330.213 | Reuters Poll: 332.686 | Surprise: 0.3340
Core CPI Index, SA: 335.423 | Prior: 334.165
CPI Wage Earner: 326.541 | Prior: 323.500

Professional Market Commentary

The BLS release showed a hot inflation mix despite headline m/m deceleration. CPI rose 0.6% m/m after 0.9% in March and 3.8% y/y after 3.3%. Core CPI rose 0.4% m/m and 2.8% y/y. Energy rose 17.9% y/y, food rose 3.2% y/y, and the core rate moved higher rather than validating a clean disinflation read.

Professional commentary in the scout tape converged on four themes: energy is the dominant headline driver; shelter strength is partly a statistical catch-up issue; airline fares and services passthrough matter as second-round risk; and the Fed cannot easily cut while core inflation is re-accelerating. The dissenting institutional view is that some of the shock may be looked through if expectations remain anchored and energy normalizes.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the decisive release of the session. The m/m headline slowed from March, but that is not enough to call stabilization because the y/y rate accelerated by 0.5pp and core inflation re-accelerated on both m/m and y/y metrics. The hierarchy is adverse: headline inflation is energy-led, but the core surprise prevents the print from being dismissed as a pure gasoline shock.

The composition is mixed but policy-negative. Energy explains a large share of the headline impulse, gasoline is the visible household shock, and food at home rose sharply enough to matter for lower-income purchasing power. Core goods are not the main problem. The core concern is services, where shelter, airline fares and other service categories keep the core impulse from fading cleanly.

The shelter detail requires care. The scout tape’s one-off catch-up framing weakens the case for extrapolating April shelter mechanically into a new rent regime. But the Fed cannot ignore the monthly core print because services ex-energy accelerated and the CPI-W / household budget channel is being hit at the same time as gasoline and food. The regime signal is not “demand overheating.” It is inflation persistence under supply shock with early services passthrough.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had already shifted to stagflation confirmation, with PCE, GDP price components, Dallas trimmed-mean PCE, ISM prices and FOMC communication all pointing to an inflation constraint that was no longer just a headline-energy issue. April CPI strengthens that path because core re-accelerates while headline energy pressure remains visible. The release moves the week’s inflation bucket from background constraint to the dominant policy signal.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This print tightens the Fed reaction-function box. It does not prove that hikes are the base case, but it raises the bar for cuts and makes any dovish repricing depend on a fast reversal in either energy, services momentum or labor data.

The market-relevant split is conditional. If PPI and import prices reinforce the pipeline impulse, the CPI print becomes part of a broader inflation pass-through sequence. If retail sales and claims weaken materially, the story shifts toward stagflation with demand destruction. If activity holds and inflation stays hot, the policy debate moves further away from 2026 cuts and toward whether the Fed must re-tighten communication.

United States — Real Weekly Earnings (Apr)
Release Date: 12 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Real Weekly Earnings MM: -0.2% | Prior: -0.9%

Professional Market Commentary

The BLS Real Earnings release is separate from CPI, even though it was released at the same 08:30 ET timestamp. The official real-earnings release covers current and real hourly and weekly earnings for private nonfarm payroll employees; broader coverage emphasized that inflation was eroding wage gains, with weekly real earnings less negative than hourly real earnings because hours helped cushion the hit.

Comment — Economist Layer

Real earnings are the bridge from CPI inflation to consumer demand quality. The monthly decline is smaller than March’s -0.9%, but the direction remains negative. That means the consumer impulse is still being squeezed in real terms even if nominal spending indicators look firm.

This is why the CPI print is more damaging than a pure market-rate event. Energy and food inflation hit the cash-flow side of the household balance sheet, while real earnings show the wage offset is insufficient. If hours partially cushion weekly pay, the pressure is softened, not removed. The signal is therefore purchasing-power deterioration rather than labor-income collapse.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive already described household demand as increasingly nominal rather than real, with real consumption weak relative to nominal spending and savings being drawn down. Real weekly earnings extends that pattern into April. The consumer remains active, but the income-quality foundation is weakening.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the consumer-stress channel that makes the CPI shock macro-relevant beyond the rates market. It does not by itself force a dovish Fed response because inflation is the reason real earnings are falling.

For the Fed, this is stagflationary, not cleanly easing-positive. A real-income squeeze can weaken consumption later, but until that shows up in retail control, claims or employment, the immediate policy read remains constrained by inflation. Retail sales is the next necessary test.

United States — PPI / Core PPI / PPI ex Food, Energy and Trade / PPI Machine Manufacturing (Apr)
Release Date: 13 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

PPI Final Demand MM: 1.4% | Prior: 0.5% | Revised: 0.7% | Reuters Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: 0.90%
PPI Final Demand YY: 6.0% | Prior: 4.0% | Revised: 4.3% | Reuters Poll: 4.9% | Surprise: 1.10%
PPI exFood/Energy MM: 1.0% | Prior: 0.1% | Revised: 0.2% | Reuters Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: 0.70%
PPI exFood/Energy YY: 5.2% | Prior: 3.8% | Revised: 4.0% | Reuters Poll: 4.3% | Surprise: 0.90%
PPI ex Food/Energy/Tr MM: 0.6% | Prior: 0.2%
PPI ex Food/Energy/Tr YY: 4.4% | Prior: 3.6% | Revised: 3.7%
PPI Machine Manuf'ing: 197.0 | Prior: 196.2

Professional Market Commentary

The official PPI release confirmed a broad wholesale inflation shock. Final demand rose 1.4% m/m in April after an upwardly revised 0.7% in March, the largest monthly gain since March 2022, and rose 6.0% y/y, the largest 12-month increase since December 2022. BLS attributed the move to both goods and services, with final demand goods up 2.0% and final demand less foods, energy and trade up 0.6% m/m and 4.4% y/y.

The scout tape’s institutional read is reinforced by the official composition: this is not just an energy headline. Trade-services margins rose, energy was large, and the narrowest core measure was also hot. Market commentary therefore shifted from “CPI may be energy-distorted” to “pipeline inflation is confirming the CPI shock.”

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the strongest deterioration signal of the week so far because it validates CPI through the wholesale channel. The headline surprise is large, but the more important point is that prior March PPI was revised higher, so the April shock is not coming off as soft a base as first reported. The cumulative signal is therefore an inflation-level shift and a momentum acceleration.

The internal hierarchy is adverse. Energy is necessary to explain the size of the move, but it is not sufficient to explain the macro significance. Goods inflation is clearly energy-led, but services are the more policy-relevant surprise. That mix points to pass-through plus margin behavior, not simply imported oil volatility.

The cleanest underlying measure is also hot. PPI ex food, energy and trade services rose 0.6% m/m and 4.4% y/y, matching the largest monthly gain since October 2025 and the strongest 12-month pace since February 2023. The release therefore broadens the inflation story from consumer energy and shelter catch-up into producer margins, freight, energy inputs and wholesale distribution.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD sequence had already moved from benign disinflation toward stagflation confirmation, with CPI, PCE, GDP prices and ISM prices pointing to a policy constraint. April PPI upgrades that path because it validates the CPI impulse through producer margins and intermediate inputs. The inflation bucket is no longer just sticky; it is broadening through the pipeline.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This release materially shifts the reaction-function balance. It does not force a near-term hike as a deterministic base case, but it makes the next Fed easing narrative extremely fragile and raises the value of any hawkish communication under the new Warsh chairmanship.

The strategic map is now conditional on three checks. First, import prices need to show whether the energy / traded-goods impulse is still flowing through the border-price channel. Second, retail sales needs to show whether consumers are absorbing the nominal shock or beginning to retrench in real terms. Third, claims need to show whether the Fed gets any labor-market justification to look through the inflation shock.

For rates and FX, the PPI block is USD-supportive through front-end policy constraint unless growth data breaks quickly. For equities, it is not immediately fatal if nominal revenue and AI capex stay resilient, but it raises discount-rate and margin-risk asymmetry. A second hot PPI print in May, especially in ex food / energy / trade, would move the inflation problem from shock absorption to domestic margin persistence.
