Advance retail and food services sales were $757.1bn, up 0.5% m/m and 4.9% y/y, with the March monthly gain revised down to 1.6% from 1.7%. Retail trade sales also rose 0.5% m/m and 5.2% y/y; nonstore retailers were up 11.1% y/y, while food services and drinking places were up 2.7% y/y. The release is not price-adjusted.

The scout tape framed the release as “forced spending versus discretionary cutback.” The headline and control group do not validate an imminent consumer collapse, but the composition is not a clean cyclical-strength signal: gasoline remains a positive nominal tailwind, online/nonstore stays firm, while several discretionary goods categories weakened. The better read is consumer spending still occurring, but increasingly price-supported and necessity-weighted.

Comment — Economist Layer

Retail sales stabilizes the growth side of the week, but it does not solve the quality problem. The headline is in line, the control group beats by 10bp, and March’s control group was revised up. That is important because it blocks a simple “inflation shock already broke the consumer” narrative.

The internal hierarchy is still darker than the headline. The retail report is nominal, not real, and it arrives after CPI, PPI, import prices and real earnings all pointed to inflation pressure on household purchasing power. Control-group resilience therefore says spending flow remains intact; it does not say real discretionary volume is healthy. The scout composition — discretionary goods weakness versus non-discretionary / online resilience — is consistent with substitution from wants to needs.

This is the consumer-side analogue of the housing pattern: stable aggregate flow, deteriorating underlying quality. The release falsifies recession-imminence, but not stagflation. The consumer is still spending, yet the spending mix is increasingly compelled by prices and necessities rather than broad discretionary confidence.

State transition: stabilization.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive already described household demand as increasingly nominal rather than real, with consumption quality weakening even as spending flows stayed positive. April retail sales extends that structure rather than reversing it. The consumer bucket remains resilient enough to delay cuts, but not strong enough to offset the inflation squeeze.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Retail sales keeps the Fed boxed. It is not weak enough to make the CPI/PPI/import-price stack look throughable, and it is not strong enough to turn the week into clean reflation.

For rates, the immediate implication is hold/hawkish-hold persistence: the demand side has not broken. For equities, the read is bifurcated: nominal revenue support remains, but discretionary-mix deterioration and real-income squeeze raise margin and volume risk. The next verification test is whether May retail control turns negative or whether discretionary categories recover. A negative May control print would start to convert the stagflationary price shock into realized demand destruction.

United States — Initial Jobless Claims / 4-Week Average / Continuing Claims (w/e 9 May / 2 May)
Release Date: 14 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Initial Jobless Clm: 211k | Prior: 200k | Revised: 199k | Reuters Poll: 205k | Surprise: 6.0k
Jobless Clm 4Wk Avg: 203.75k | Prior: 203.25k | Revised: 203.00k
Cont Jobless Clm: 1.782M | Prior: 1.766M | Revised: 1.758M | Reuters Poll: 1.790M | Surprise: -0.008M

Professional Market Commentary

The DOL release showed initial claims rising to 211k from a downwardly revised 199k, while the four-week average rose only 750 to 203.75k. Continuing claims rose to 1.782M from a downwardly revised 1.758M, and the four-week continuing-claims average declined. On an unadjusted basis, initial claims were 190,571 versus 203,579 in the comparable week of 2025.

The professional read is “firm but not accelerating labor stress.” The weekly claims beat is directionally softer, but the level remains low, the four-week average is stable, and continuing claims are still below poll. This preserves the low-hire / low-fire labor regime rather than shifting the tape toward labor-market breakdown.

Comment — Economist Layer

Claims are the key second-mandate check after CPI/PPI. The increase to 211k matters directionally, but it is not enough to change the labor state. The 4-week average at 203.75k is still low, continuing claims are below consensus, and the unadjusted comparison versus the prior year remains favorable.

The important distinction is marginal momentum versus level. Momentum softened slightly because initial claims rose and the weekly print beat consensus. But the level is still consistent with a labor market that is too firm to give the Fed an easing trigger. Continuing claims are also not sending a duration-of-unemployment warning yet.

This release therefore protects the hawkish-hold configuration. Inflation has deteriorated; labor has not deteriorated enough to offset it. The Fed can acknowledge labor cooling at the margin, but it cannot pivot on this claims print.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had labor as a policy-constraint pillar, with low claims and firm compensation preventing a clean cutting signal. This week’s claims keep that pillar intact. The marginal rise does not yet alter the labor bucket from resilient to deteriorating.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Claims are not dovish enough. They preserve the “inflation hot, labor not broken” reaction function and therefore increase the asymmetry against near-term cuts.

The market trigger is now clear: the 4-week average needs to move through the 220k area and stay there before labor can compete with the inflation stack. Continuing claims above 1.85M would provide secondary confirmation. Until then, low claims allow the Fed to prioritize inflation risk, especially after CPI/PPI/import-price acceleration.

United States — Import Prices / Export Prices (Apr)
Release Date: 14 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Import Prices MM: 1.9% | Prior: 0.8% | Revised: 0.9% | Reuters Poll: 1.0% | Surprise: 0.90%
Import Prices YY: 4.2% | Prior: 2.1% | Revised: 2.3%
Export Prices MM: 3.3% | Prior: 1.6% | Revised: 1.5% | Reuters Poll: 1.1% | Surprise: 2.20%

Professional Market Commentary

The BLS International Price Program release confirmed a major external-price shock. Import prices rose 1.9% m/m after a revised 0.9% March increase, and were up 4.2% y/y, the largest 12-month advance since October 2022. Fuel imports rose 16.3% m/m, the largest monthly gain since March 2022; nonfuel imports rose 0.8% m/m; and export prices rose 3.3% m/m, with both agricultural and nonagricultural exports contributing.

The scout tape correctly framed this as the third inflation pipeline after CPI and PPI: consumer prices, producer prices, and now border prices all moved in the wrong direction. The official details also weaken the “all oil” explanation because nonfuel imports rose materially and imports from China rose 0.8% m/m, the largest one-month increase since July 2008. Import prices from Canada rose 5.6% m/m, the largest monthly rise since March 2022.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the external confirmation of the week’s inflation deterioration. CPI showed the household price shock; PPI showed the wholesale / margin pipeline; import prices show the external supply channel. The size of the import-price surprise and the upward revision to March mean the imported inflation impulse is not rolling off yet.

The internal hierarchy matters. Fuel explains the largest part of the move and is the most direct Iran/Hormuz transmission channel. But nonfuel imports at +0.8% m/m, capital goods, industrial supplies, consumer goods ex-autos, and China import prices all point to broader tradable-goods pressure. This is analytically important because CPI core goods were still near-flat in April; import-price acceleration raises the risk that core-goods disinflation fades with a lag.

Export prices also matter more than usual. A +3.3% m/m export-price gain is not a consumer-demand indicator, but it signals that energy, agricultural and nonagricultural price pressure is affecting the external terms-of-trade layer. The Canada and China locality signals deserve tracking because they separate energy-heavy regional price pressure from potentially broader supply-chain repricing.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had already moved away from a clean domestic-disinflation story, with tariff, supply-chain, energy and ISM-price pressure feeding the policy constraint. April import prices extend that pressure through the external-price channel. The inflation regime is now broader than domestic services and energy-sensitive CPI.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Import prices make the Fed problem harder, not easier. A central bank can lean against demand, but it has less traction against imported fuel and supply-chain price shocks without imposing collateral damage on growth.

For rates and FX, this strengthens the front-end constraint and supports USD through relative policy rigidity unless the growth side breaks. For equities, it raises input-cost and margin risk, particularly where pricing power is not strong enough to pass through imported cost pressure. The next trigger is May import prices: a move back toward +0.4% m/m or lower would support stabilization; another ≥+1.0% m/m would move the external channel toward outright escalation.

United States — Business Inventories (Mar)
Release Date: 14 May 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Business Inventories MM: 0.9% | Prior: 0.4% | Reuters Poll: 0.8% | Surprise: 0.10%

Professional Market Commentary

The Census Manufacturing and Trade Inventories and Sales release showed total business inventories rising 0.9% m/m to $2.7097tn in March, up 2.0% y/y. Sales rose 2.1% m/m and 7.1% y/y, while the inventory/sales ratio fell to 1.32 from 1.33 in February and 1.38 a year earlier. The table detail shows manufacturers’ inventories up 0.6%, retailers up 0.6%, and merchant wholesalers up 1.3%.

The tape read is neutral-to-constructive for activity arithmetic. The inventory build is large, but the sales side is also strong enough to prevent the release from looking like involuntary accumulation.

Comment — Economist Layer

Business inventories are a lower-tier but useful GDP-quality check. The +0.9% inventory build is large and slightly above consensus, but the simultaneous 2.1% sales increase changes the interpretation. A build with falling sales would be recessionary; a build with firm sales is more accommodative and may reflect precautionary stockbuilding, supply-chain hedging or simply nominal sales strength.

The inventory/sales ratio is the key signal. At 1.32, down from 1.33 in February and below the prior-year ratio, inventories are not outrunning sales. Wholesale leads the build, which fits the broader week’s supply-chain / import-price / PPI story, but the ratio prevents a clean involuntary-overhang call.

The limitation is nominal distortion. Sales and inventories are not price-adjusted, so part of the strength can be inflation arithmetic. Still, this is not a growth-break release. It supports moderate activity with price-distorted nominal strength.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive already treated inventories as a near-term growth support with possible front-loading characteristics rather than clean final-demand strength. March business inventories keep that interpretation intact. The build supports activity arithmetic but does not remove the risk of later overhang if real demand weakens.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Low-to-medium standalone policy relevance. The release helps the growth-resilience side and prevents the week from becoming a pure demand-collapse tape.

For the Fed, the effect is indirectly hawkish because inventories and sales do not show a break in activity. But the strategic risk is temporal: if inventories are precautionary against supply disruption and final demand softens later, today’s support can become tomorrow’s drag. Watch ISM inventories/new orders and May retail control for confirmation.

FRIDAY 15 MAY 2026

United States — NY Fed Empire State Manufacturing Survey (May)
Release Date: 15 May 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

NY Fed Manufacturing: 19.60 | Prior: 11.00 | Reuters Poll: 7.50 | Surprise: 12.10

Professional Market Commentary

The NY Fed release showed a strong regional manufacturing beat. The headline general business conditions index rose to 19.6, its highest reading since April 2022, while new orders rose to 22.7, the highest level in more than four years. Shipments stayed positive at 18.9; delivery times lengthened to 20.4, a four-year high; supply availability remained negative at -10.7; and both price indexes moved to their highest levels since 2022.

The scout tape’s read is the correct one: the activity inflection is real, but it is occurring inside the stagflation regime rather than against it. Strong orders, positive shipments and improved future conditions keep the growth-resilience leg alive, while prices paid at 62.6 and prices received at 31.8 confirm acute cost pressure and pass-through.

Comment — Economist Layer

Empire State is a high-signal survey because it resolves the week’s main contradiction in favor of “activity resilient, inflation worse,” not “inflation worse because activity collapsed.” The headline surprise is large, new orders are at a multi-year high, and future conditions improved. This blocks a simple manufacturing-recession read.

The composition is still stagflationary. Prices paid and prices received both accelerated sharply, delivery times lengthened, supply availability worsened, and inventories rose. This is not a benign demand rebound; it is a regional manufacturing expansion occurring alongside supply stress and cost pass-through. Labor inputs also do not show a clean expansion surge: employment and hours remain positive, but the scout detail flags modest softening relative to April.

The analytical hierarchy is therefore: demand strong, supply constrained, prices accelerating. That is exactly the configuration that keeps the Fed boxed. Empire State is not a national hard-data release, but it provides the first regional survey confirmation that the CPI/PPI/import-price shock is visible in manufacturing micro-data.

State transition: stabilization / continuation.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive already treated manufacturing as mixed but increasingly constrained by prices, inventories and supply-chain pressure. Empire State extends that pattern with a stronger activity leg and a hotter price leg. The manufacturing bucket improves on growth, but deteriorates on inflation quality.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Empire State is hawkish in reaction-function terms because it denies the Fed an activity-break offset. The data says manufacturing demand can rebound even as input and selling-price pressure intensifies.

For rates and FX, this supports front-end policy rigidity and USD resilience unless national activity data contradicts it. For equities, the mix is ambiguous: better demand helps cyclicals, but higher prices paid, longer delivery times and worsening supply availability raise margin and operating-risk pressure. The next test is whether June Empire State keeps the headline above 10 while prices paid remain above 55; that would harden the stagflation-confirmation call.

United States — Industrial Production / Capacity Utilization / Manufacturing Output (Apr)
Release Date: 15 May 2026 | Local Time: 15:15
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Industrial Production MM: 0.7% | Prior: -0.5% | Revised: -0.3% | Reuters Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: 0.40%
Capacity Utilization SA: 76.1% | Prior: 75.7% | Reuters Poll: 75.8% | Surprise: 0.30%
Manuf Output MM: 0.6% | Prior: -0.1% | Revised: 0.1% | Reuters Poll: 0.2% | Surprise: 0.40%

Professional Market Commentary

The Fed G.17 release confirmed a hard-activity rebound. Industrial production rose 0.7% in April after a revised 0.3% March decline, manufacturing output rose 0.6%, mining edged down 0.1%, utilities rose 1.9%, and manufacturing excluding motor vehicles and parts rose 0.3%. Total IP stood 1.4% above its year-earlier level, while capacity utilization rose to 76.1%, still 3.3pp below its 1972–2025 long-run average.

The tape read is activity resilience with slack. April’s beat and upward March revisions strengthen the growth side, while the capacity-utilization level prevents a pure overheating interpretation. Business equipment rose 1.5%, transit equipment rose 4.2%, and consumer goods rose 0.9%, but manufacturing excluding autos was only +0.3%, so the rebound is real but partly concentrated.

Comment — Economist Layer

Industrial production is the hard-data confirmation that the growth side did not break during the inflation shock week. The headline beat, manufacturing beat, capacity-utilization beat, and upward March revisions all point in the same direction: the March soft patch was less severe than first reported, and April activity rebounded more strongly than expected.

The internal hierarchy is constructive but not inflation-clean. Manufacturing output rose 0.6%, but ex-autos rose 0.3%, so motor vehicles contributed meaningfully. Utilities added 1.9%, which may reflect weather and energy-demand effects rather than broad industrial momentum. Business equipment at +1.5% is the more forward-looking positive signal because it suggests capex activity has not rolled over.

Capacity utilization is the constraint on the hawkish interpretation. At 76.1%, total utilization remains 3.3pp below its long-run average, and manufacturing utilization at 75.8% remains 2.4pp below its long-run average. That means the industrial sector still contains slack even as output rebounds. The signal is therefore not “overheating.” It is “activity resilient enough to prevent cuts, but not tight enough to validate aggressive hikes.”

State transition: stabilization.

Context from prior regional live file: The prior USD archive had treated industrial activity as mixed, with soft patches offset by inventory support, capex pockets and price-pressure resilience. April IP shifts the activity leg firmer by revising away part of the March weakness and delivering a broad enough rebound. The growth bucket stabilizes, while the inflation bucket remains the dominant constraint.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the release that closes the week’s Fed box. Inflation is broadening, labor is not breaking, and now hard industrial activity has rebounded. That combination makes the easing case weak.

But the release also limits the hike case. Capacity utilization is still materially below long-run norms, and the manufacturing rebound has some auto and utilities concentration. The Fed’s best description is therefore “stuck”: hold/hawkish-hold, with communication risk skewed toward removing easing bias rather than pre-committing to hikes.

For markets, IP is USD- and front-end-supportive through the no-cuts channel. For equities, it supports nominal activity and capex resilience, but it does not neutralize the margin pressure implied by PPI, import prices and Empire State prices paid. A May IP decline would reopen the growth-break channel; utilization above 77% would harden the slack-absorption / inflation-persistence concern.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Inflation has broadened across CPI, PPI, import prices and regional manufacturing prices, while labor, retail flow and industrial activity have not broken.
Why it matters: The Fed’s reaction function is constrained by multi-channel inflation, but the growth data are still resilient and industrial slack persists.
Implication: The week supports hawkish hold / possible tightening communication risk, not a clean hike regime and not an easing regime.

Import Prices / Export Prices (14 May 2026)

Industrial Production / Capacity Utilization / Manufacturing Output (15 May 2026)
Why it matters: Hard-data activity confirmation. The rebound and upward revisions close the week by showing growth resilience, while low utilization prevents a pure overheating interpretation.
PPI / Core PPI / PPI ex Food, Energy and Trade (13 May 2026)

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 27 Apr 2026 to 1 May 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The US week completed a stagflation-confirmation regime rather than a clean recession or clean growth tape. Growth stayed mechanically resilient through core capex, inventories, GDP, claims and S&P manufacturing, but the quality of that resilience deteriorated: household demand was increasingly nominal rather than real, housing breadth weakened, Chicago PMI flagged regional downside, and manufacturing strength was driven by defensive stockpiling rather than clean final demand. ISM confirmed this on Friday: the headline stayed expansionary at 52.7 and new orders improved to 54.1, but prices paid surged to 84.6 and employment fell to 46.4, turning the manufacturing confirmation into a higher-quality stagflation signal rather than a clean growth upgrade. Labor remained too firm to validate cuts, with initial claims at 189k and ECI still firm, while inflation pressure escalated through PCE, GDP price components, Dallas trimmed-mean PCE, survey prices and FOMC communication.

The final policy signal was hawkish hold / delayed cuts. The FOMC’s mechanical hold was expected, but the four-dissent vote, three hawkish objections to easing-bias language, PCE acceleration, Q1 core PCE prices at 4.3%, trimmed-mean PCE at 2.9%, and ISM prices paid at 84.6 moved the weekly regime from data-provider stagflation concern to central-bank and national-accounts confirmation. The week’s core tension is therefore not weak growth alone, but resilient nominal activity plus weakening real-quality indicators plus persistent inflation: enough activity to delay cuts, enough inflation to keep the Fed constrained, enough forward-growth damage to prevent a clean hawkish-growth story.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
GDP rose 2.0% in Q1, core capital goods orders surged 3.3% m/m, inventories were supportive, S&P manufacturing reached 54.5, and ISM held in expansion at 52.7. But consumer spending decelerated in GDP, real consumption rose only 0.2% m/m, housing permits fell sharply, Case-Shiller broadened deterioration, Chicago PMI dropped below 50, and S&P manufacturing strength was explicitly stockpiling-driven.

Labor: +
Supporting evidence:
Initial claims fell to 189k, continuing claims dropped to 1.785M, and the firing-side signal remained exceptionally strong. ECI rose 0.9% q/q, wages rose 0.8%, and benefits accelerated to 1.2%, leaving labor too resilient and compensation too firm to provide the Fed with a clean easing trigger. Manufacturing employment softened in both S&P and ISM, but not enough to dominate the broader claims signal.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
Headline PCE rose 0.7% m/m and 3.5% y/y, core PCE rose to 3.2% y/y, Q1 core PCE prices accelerated to 4.3%, Dallas Fed trimmed-mean PCE jumped to 2.9%, and ISM prices paid surged to 84.6. Dallas Fed manufacturing prices, S&P input/output prices, GDP price components and FOMC communication all confirmed that inflation pressure was no longer just a headline-energy issue.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Mortgage rates remained restrictive near 6.37%, housing affordability stayed binding, permits weakened, and the FOMC outcome pushed against dovish repricing. Financial conditions did not tighten through one single market print in the archive, but the policy communication, inflation stack and mortgage-rate backdrop kept the effective macro-financial impulse restrictive.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The FOMC held as expected, but the four-dissent vote and three hawkish objections to easing-bias language made the meeting the central weekly regime event. Strong claims, firm capex, positive GDP and still-expansionary ISM prevent a clean cut case, while PCE, GDP prices, trimmed-mean PCE and ISM prices paid prevent the Fed from looking through inflation. The reaction function is boxed into hawkish hold unless labor or real demand deteriorates more visibly.

United States — Conference Board Consumer Confidence (Apr)
Release Date: 28 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 10:00 ET
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Consumer Confidence: 92.8 | Prior: 91.8 | Revised: 92.2 | Poll: 89.0 | Surprise: +3.80

Professional Market Commentary

The Conference Board reported that confidence edged up to 92.8 from an upwardly revised 92.2, while the Present Situation Index slipped to 123.8 and the Expectations Index rose to 72.2. The official framing was explicitly cautious: confidence was “overall little changed,” with consumers still worried about gasoline, prices and the Middle East conflict. (conference-board.org
)

Comment — Economist Layer

This is stabilization, not a clean confidence recovery. The headline beat is real, but the level remains low, expectations are still below the recession-warning threshold, and current business conditions deteriorated.

The key reconciliation is labor versus prices. Conference Board is more labor-market-sensitive than Michigan; it improved because job perceptions and income expectations stabilized. Michigan remained much weaker because price and cost-of-living anxiety dominates that survey. The correct read is not contradiction but household bifurcation: secure enough in employment, still pressured by inflation.

Survey timing matters. The 1–22 April window captured the ceasefire/equity-rebound period, so the print may overstate underlying confidence if geopolitical and energy stress re-intensifies afterward.

State transition: stabilization

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD archive had already shown crisis-low Michigan sentiment and rising medium-term inflation expectations, while claims stayed benign. Conference Board stabilizes the labor-confidence leg but does not reverse the price-stress story. The consumer regime remains employment-supported but inflation-constrained.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The release does not relieve the Fed. It weakens the near-term recession narrative at the margin because labor perceptions improved, but it also reinforces why the Fed cannot take weak sentiment alone as an easing trigger.

The policy implication is bidirectional optionality. Stronger labor confidence and nominal spending delay cuts; price anxiety and weak expectations cap the growth story. The next verification tests are PCE, claims and the official labor report.

United States — FOMC Rate Decision / IOER
Release Date: 29 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:00 ET
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Fed Funds Target Rate: 3.5-3.75% | Prior: 3.5-3.75% | Poll: 3.5-3.75% | Surprise: 0.0000%
Fed Interest on Excess Reserves: 3.65% | Prior: 3.65%

Professional Market Commentary

The Fed held the target range at 3.50–3.75% and explicitly noted that Middle East developments were contributing to high uncertainty. The official statement kept data-dependence and the language on “extent and timing” of additional adjustments, but the vote and commentary were far more important than the mechanical hold. (federalreserve.gov
)

Reuters framed the meeting as the most divided since 1992: four officials dissented, with Stephen Miran preferring a 25bp cut and Hammack, Kashkari and Logan objecting to the easing-bias language rather than to the hold itself. That makes the meeting a hawkish hold despite the unchanged rate and preserved easing-bias wording. (reuters.com
)

Comment — Economist Layer

The mechanical decision was fully expected; the analytical content was committee fragmentation and communication. A 4-dissent vote is not normal noise. Three dissenters pushed against easing-bias language, which means the center of gravity moved more hawkish even though the formal statement still preserved optionality.

The Fed is now openly describing the policy problem as a supply-shock dilemma: growth downside risk and inflation upside risk are rising together. That is exactly the reaction-function bind implied by the week’s data: resilient capex and claims, softer housing and confidence, hotter PCE and survey prices.

The key distinction is between the rate decision and the path signal. The rate was unchanged, but the path signal became less dovish because the hawkish dissent cluster challenged the idea that the next move should still be implicitly biased toward cuts.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD archive had already moved to a delayed-cuts / hawkish-hold regime because inflation expectations and PMI prices worsened while layoffs stayed benign. The FOMC validates that regime at the policy level. The policy constraint has moved from market inference to visible committee disagreement.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the central event of the week. The correct read is not “hold as expected”; it is “hold with hawkish fragmentation.”

For rates and USD, the meeting raises the bar for dovish repricing. Cuts require clearer labor deterioration or a sharp reversal in inflation; hikes still require broader inflation persistence and resilience, but the three hawkish communication dissents show the easing bias is fragile. The next policy risk is removal of easing-bias language if PCE, trimmed-mean PCE and inflation expectations stay firm.

This locks in the hawkish-hold baseline. Core PCE at 3.2% y/y and headline PCE at 3.5% y/y are not compatible with an easy near-term cut narrative while claims are at cycle lows and capex is strong.

The dovish argument must now come from forward deterioration: real spending, savings depletion, labor cracks or ISM weakness. Without those, the Fed is boxed into waiting even if growth quality is worsening.

FRIDAY 1 MAY 2026

United States — S&P Global Manufacturing PMI Final (Apr)
Release Date: 1 May 2026 | Local Time: 09:45 ET
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

S&P Global Manufacturing PMI Final: 54.5 | Prior: 54.0

Professional Market Commentary

S&P Global revised the April manufacturing PMI final to 54.5 from the 54.0 flash estimate, up from 52.3 in March and the strongest manufacturing expansion since May 2022. The official release explicitly discounted the headline strength: production and new orders rose at the fastest pace in four years, but the gains were heavily linked to stock-building ahead of feared price rises, supply shortages and Middle East-related disruption. (pmi.spglobal.com
)

Williamson’s commentary is the key market tape. He stated that the manufacturing surge was “not the cause for cheer” that the headline suggested, because companies were trying to get ahead of price increases and supply shortages. He also highlighted that shipments, orders and production were boosted by stock-building, especially among larger firms with deeper pockets, while employment fell and selling prices rose as firms protected margins. (pmi.spglobal.com
)

Comment — Economist Layer

This print resolves the Chicago PMI tension in favor of continued national manufacturing front-loading rather than confirmed national manufacturing reversal. Chicago PMI had warned that the front-loading cycle might be exhausting, but the S&P final moved up from the flash estimate and extended the sample through 27 April, showing that the stockpiling impulse was still building later in the month.

The headline strength is therefore not a clean growth upgrade. Output and orders are strong mechanically, but the motivation is defensive: firms are buying inputs, raising inventories and accelerating production to manage supply-chain and price risk. The employment detail cuts the other way: manufacturing employment fell for the first time in nine months and by the most in a year and a half, which makes the release a classic stagflationary manufacturing print — strong output, softer labor, rising prices.

The larger-firm concentration is important. Stock-building by firms with “deeper pockets” suggests the manufacturing boost is uneven and balance-sheet-dependent. Smaller firms are more exposed to input-cost pressure and less able to pre-stock, which reinforces the bifurcation theme already visible in the consumer and capex tape.

State transition: continuation

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD archive had already treated manufacturing strength as front-loaded and inventory-driven rather than clean final-demand acceleration. The S&P final confirms that interpretation and prevents the Chicago PMI downside surprise from becoming the dominant national signal. Manufacturing remains mechanically strong, but the quality of strength is defensive and inflationary rather than cleanly cyclical.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This strengthens the hawkish-hold regime, not a bullish growth regime. The release says manufacturing is still expanding, but for the wrong reasons: inventory insurance, supply-chain stress and price anticipation.

For the Fed, the print supports patience. Strong output and orders reduce urgency to cut, while falling employment and rising input/output prices keep the stagflation bind alive. ISM remains the next verification test, but after this release Chicago PMI should be treated as regional warning rather than national reversal.

United States — ISM Manufacturing PMI / Prices Paid / Employment / New Orders (Apr)
Release Date: 1 May 2026 | Local Time: 10:00 ET
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

ISM Manufacturing PMI: 52.7 | Prior: 52.7 | Poll: 53.0 | Surprise: -0.30
ISM Manufacturing Prices Paid: 84.6 | Prior: 78.3 | Poll: 80.0 | Surprise: +4.60
ISM Manufacturing Employment Index: 46.4 | Prior: 48.7 | Poll: 49.0 | Surprise: -2.60
ISM Manufacturing New Orders Index: 54.1 | Prior: 53.5

Professional Market Commentary

The ISM release confirmed the S&P Global manufacturing signal rather than the Chicago PMI warning. The headline PMI held at 52.7, slightly below consensus but still expansionary, while new orders improved to 54.1 and prices paid surged to 84.6, the highest level since April 2022. Employment deteriorated to 46.4, reinforcing the same stagflationary manufacturing mix already visible in the S&P final: activity mechanically resilient, prices accelerating, labor softening.

The key committee commentary was consistent with the stockpiling thesis. ISM survey commentary pointed to firms ordering ahead of price increases, which corroborates Williamson’s S&P framing that April manufacturing strength was driven by defensive buying, supply-chain risk and price anticipation rather than clean final-demand acceleration.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the verification test for the Chicago PMI tension, and it resolves that tension in favor of national manufacturing continuation rather than national manufacturing reversal. Chicago PMI at 49.2 remains a valid regional warning, but ISM holding at 52.7 and new orders rising to 54.1 mean the national manufacturing cycle had not yet rolled over.

The inflation detail is the dominant signal. Prices paid at 84.6 is a major escalation and confirms that the manufacturing strength is inflationary in quality. This is not a benign expansion: new orders are still expanding, but firms are paying sharply higher prices and employment is contracting.

The employment decline matters because it aligns with S&P Global’s report that manufacturing employment fell for the first time in nine months and by the most in a year and a half. The manufacturing sector is therefore not cleanly accelerating; it is producing a late-cycle stagflationary configuration of resilient orders, aggressive input-price pressure and weaker labor demand.

State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD archive had already treated manufacturing strength as front-loaded and inventory-driven rather than clean final-demand acceleration. ISM confirms that the Chicago PMI contraction was not yet a national reversal, but it also strengthens the inflation-quality warning through the prices-paid surge. Manufacturing therefore remains mechanically resilient, but the quality of resilience is increasingly policy-unfriendly.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is hawkish for the reaction function. ISM does not validate recession pricing because the headline and new orders remain expansionary, but it does validate stagflation pricing because prices paid are extremely hot and employment is weakening.

For the Fed, this supports delayed cuts and keeps the easing-bias language fragile. The release confirms that the manufacturing side is not yet weak enough to force policy relief, while the price side is strong enough to keep hawks engaged.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Manufacturing activity is still mechanically strong, but the strength is being generated by defensive stockpiling, supply disruption and price anticipation rather than clean final demand.

Why it matters: ISM confirmed that Chicago PMI was a regional warning rather than a national rollover, but the confirmation came through prices paid at 84.6 and employment at 46.4, making the signal more stagflationary than growth-positive.

Implication: Manufacturing keeps recession timing pushed out, but worsens the Fed-quality signal: resilient orders, softening employment, and extreme price pressure reinforce hawkish hold rather than clean growth optimism.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

FOMC Rate Decision / IOER (29 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: The mechanical hold was expected, but the four dissents and three hawkish communication objections turned the meeting into a regime-confirmation event. The Fed’s easing bias is now visibly fragile.

Personal Income / Consumption / PCE / Core PCE (30 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: The monthly PCE release confirmed the nominal-real wedge: nominal spending strong, real consumption modest, saving rate falling and core PCE accelerating to 3.2% y/y. This is the cleanest consumer-side stagflation signal.

GDP Advance / GDP Price Components (30 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: Q1 GDP stayed positive but missed consensus while price components accelerated sharply. This validates the stagflation read at the national-accounts level and confirms that the Fed’s problem is not weak growth alone, but weak growth quality plus persistent inflation.

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The US week confirmed a stagflationary resilience regime rather than a clean growth acceleration. Retail control strength, firmer pending home sales, benign claims and broad PMI beats kept the activity floor intact, but the quality of growth deteriorated: manufacturing strength was front-loaded and inventory/safety-stock driven, services were only modest, housing remained rate-constrained, and sentiment stayed crisis-low despite the final Michigan rebound.

The binding policy signal was inflation and expectations rather than labor stress. PMI price measures moved back into multi-year-high territory, Michigan 5-year expectations rose to 3.5%, and labor firing data remained too benign to give the Fed a clear easing trigger. The weekly regime is therefore delayed-cuts / hawkish-hold: positive nominal activity, weaker real-quality internals, emerging hiring softness, and a more binding inflation-expectations constraint.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Retail sales and the control group beat decisively, business inventories added modest GDP support, and S&P Global manufacturing and composite PMIs surprised to the upside. But the growth signal was not clean: PMI strength was explicitly tied to safety-stock and emergency buying, services remained modest, CFNAI stayed below trend, and pending home sales looked like stabilization from a weak base rather than a housing recovery.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Initial claims at 214k and continuing claims at 1.821M kept firing-side labor stress contained. The deterioration came from forward labor internals rather than layoffs: PMI employment components pointed to slowing hiring momentum, manufacturing headcount weakness and only marginal overall employment growth. The week preserved a cooling-without-cracking labor regime.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:

The slight easing in 1-year Michigan expectations did not offset the medium-term de-anchoring risk.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
Mortgage rates eased 7bp to 6.35%, which helped the conditional housing-stabilization read, and pending sales surprised positively. But the rate level remained restrictive, affordability and lock-in constraints persisted, and the broader week did not show enough easing in financing conditions to validate a durable housing or demand turn.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The Fed constraint tightened because inflation and expectations worsened while activity and labor did not weaken enough to justify near-term easing. Retail control strength, PMI price pressure and the 5-year Michigan expectations rise push against cuts; services softness, weak sentiment and hiring-side cooling keep renewed tightening off the table. The policy mix is hawkish hold / delayed cuts.

DATA WINDOW: 13 Apr 2026 to 16 Apr 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The 13–16 April U.S. tape resolved into a firmer stagflation-transmission mix rather than a clean recession break. Growth weakened through housing, small-business confidence, and hard industrial activity: existing-home sales fell to a nine-month low, builder sentiment deteriorated sharply, NFIB optimism dropped as uncertainty jumped, and industrial production softened from a stronger revised February base. But labor did not crack: initial claims stayed low, continuing claims only edged higher, and Philly Fed orders and shipments surprised strongly even as employment turned negative. Inflation pressure remained the binding policy problem. PPI and import prices both delivered softer headline optics than feared, but the underlying goods, energy, and nonfuel import pipeline stayed firm enough to keep March PCE tracking elevated. The resulting regime is weaker real activity in rate-sensitive sectors, no decisive labor-market deterioration, and an inflation pipeline still too firm to open a clear easing lane.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
The week’s dominant growth signal was softer. Existing-home sales fell to 3.98M from a revised 4.13M and HMI dropped to 34, confirming housing deterioration across resale and builder channels. NFIB optimism fell to 95.8 as uncertainty rose to 92, extending confidence weakness into the small-business sector. Industrial production fell 0.5% m/m and manufacturing output slipped 0.1%, broadening the slowdown beyond housing. Redbook stayed positive and Philly Fed activity was strong, but those offsets did not overturn the week’s weaker hard-growth mix.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Labor stayed resilient enough to avoid a recessionary signal, but the week added some caution on hiring and labor demand. Initial claims fell to 207k and the four-week average stayed near 210k, keeping layoffs low. Continuing claims rose to 1.818M, consistent with slower re-employment rather than labor breakage. Philly Fed employment fell to -5.1 and NFIB uncertainty rose sharply, suggesting weaker hiring appetite beneath stable separations. The labor bucket therefore remained cooling without cracking.

Inflation: +
Supporting evidence:
The inflation signal stayed uncomfortably firm. PPI headline softness was driven heavily by gasoline and flat services, but final-demand goods rose 1.6% m/m and the non-energy core was softer rather than cleanly benign. Import prices also flattered on the headline while fuel and nonfuel imported-cost pressure remained firm, with nonfuel imports up 0.6% m/m and 2.8% y/y. Philly Fed prices paid and received moved higher as well. The week did not show generalized inflation re-acceleration everywhere, but it clearly failed to deliver a convincing pipeline-cooling break.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Financial conditions continued to bite in rate-sensitive sectors rather than ease. Existing-home sales and builder sentiment both deteriorated under renewed mortgage-rate pressure, poor affordability, and weak confidence, showing tighter conditions still transmitting into housing. NFIB uncertainty rose sharply, consistent with a more cautious operating and financing backdrop for smaller firms. No release in the file suggested an easing in financing stress strong enough to offset those channels.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The week tightened the Fed’s constraint rather than loosening it. Inflation pipeline data remained firm enough to keep March PCE tracking elevated, while claims stayed too stable to provide a clear labor-market excuse for easing. Philly Fed also combined stronger activity with hotter price measures, reinforcing the growth-versus-inflation bind instead of resolving it. The weekly regime therefore points to prolonged hold, with the bar still high for both hikes and cuts.

NAR’s cut to its 2026 existing-home sales growth forecast, now 4% instead of the prior 14%, reinforces that this is no longer being treated as a temporary wobble.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior frozen USD sequence had already shown housing as a weak, rate-sensitive downside channel rather than a recovering demand engine. March existing-home sales confirms that trajectory and intensifies it through a sharper transaction drop despite a firmer February base. The housing bucket therefore remains a downside growth channel without yet delivering meaningful price-clearing disinflation.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For macro mapping, this is a clean growth-downside transmission channel rather than a direct Fed mover on its own. It strengthens the case that tighter financial conditions are now biting in the most mortgage-sensitive part of the economy, but because prices remain sticky, housing is not delivering the kind of fast disinflation relief that would ease the policy constraint. PPI ex Food/Energy MM: 0.1% | Prior: 0.5% | Revised: 0.3% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: -0.40%
PPI ex Food/Energy/Trade YY: 3.6% | Prior: 3.5%
PPI ex Food/Energy/Trade MM: 0.2% | Prior: 0.5%
PPI Machine Manufacturing: 196.2 | Prior: 195.9 | Revised: 195.8.

Professional Market Commentary

The institutional tape treated this as a soft print with a harder underlying story. Official BLS details showed headline pressure came overwhelmingly from goods, especially energy and gasoline, while final-demand services were unchanged; the public market read was tactical relief against expectations, not a clean inflation-clear signal. Reuters also noted economists still saw core PCE around 0.3% m/m and 3.2% y/y, the highest in two years, despite the softer producer print.

Comment — Economist Layer

The internal hierarchy matters more than the headline miss. Final-demand goods rose 1.6% m/m, with nearly half of that rise tied to gasoline, while final-demand services were flat because trade margins narrowed; that makes the 0.1% m/m core ex food and energy a genuinely softer composition than the headline would suggest.

JGM note: core producer prices rose only 0.1% m/m versus 0.5% consensus, partly because trade-service margins narrowed and partly because core goods prices were only modestly firm. That is a useful sign that tariff-related pressure may be easing at the margin and that pipeline inflation is not yet running out of control, even if March core PCE still looks firmer than the PPI core alone would imply.

The clean read is therefore not “inflation problem solved,” but “pipeline shock confirmed while the non-energy core remains more contained than feared.” That buys the Fed tactical breathing room for March, even if the April-May window stays harder should services begin to absorb fuel and logistics costs.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior frozen USD week had already tightened the inflation constraint through ISM prices paid, headline CPI, and higher household inflation expectations, even as core and median measures looked less extreme. March PPI confirms that split by showing an official upstream goods-and-energy shock without a full services breakout yet. The regime therefore remains a harder stagflation bind, but not a fully generalized inflation re-acceleration across all core buckets.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

JGM policy key: the Fed still needs clearer evidence that core goods inflation is decelerating enough to prove the earlier tariff-style pressure was temporary before it can comfortably look through a new oil shock. The hurdle to hike is also fairly high absent a real move in inflation expectations or a broader inflation re-acceleration, because policy is already near estimates of neutral and another directional move could be costly; but that same logic keeps the bar for cuts high as well, especially after prior easing and with March core PCE still tracking firm. So this release does not open an easing lane and does not force an immediate hiking response: it reinforces a prolonged hold with a high evidentiary threshold in both directions.

WEDNESDAY 15 APR 2026

United States — Import Price Index / Export Price Index (Mar)
Release Date: 15 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 08:30 ET
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Import Prices MM: 0.8% | Prior: 1.3% | Revised: 0.9% | Poll: 2.0% | Surprise: -1.20%
Import Prices YY: 2.1% | Prior: 1.3% | Revised: 1.0%
Export Prices MM: 1.6% | Prior: 1.5% | Revised: 1.9% | Poll: 1.5% | Surprise: 0.10%.

Professional Market Commentary

The public institutional framing was that the soft headline import print flattered the inflation picture. Reuters emphasized survey-timing effects in oil capture, while the official BLS release still showed firm imported inflation pressure through fuel, nonfuel industrial supplies, capital goods, and locality measures such as China and Mexico. Reuters also said the combined CPI, PPI, and import-price data were pushing March PCE tracking materially higher.

Comment — Economist Layer

The important signal is beneath the headline miss. Fuel import prices rose 2.9% m/m, nonfuel import prices rose 0.6% m/m and 2.8% y/y, the largest twelve-month increase since October 2022, and BLS showed firmer prices in industrial supplies, capital goods, and imports from China. So March did not deliver a clean imported-inflation break at all: it delivered a softer-than-feared top line while the underlying nonfuel imported-cost structure kept firming.

State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior frozen USD week had already shifted the inflation story toward an energy-led headline shock with firmer producer-price pressure underneath. March import prices extend that same trajectory by showing that imported nonfuel cost pressure is also moving higher even though the headline monthly surprise looked soft. The regime implication is that external price transmission remains firmer than the top-line miss suggests.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This reinforces the same policy key as PPI. A soft headline miss is not enough for the Fed if the underlying goods pipeline is still firm and March PCE is tracking around 0.3% m/m core and 3.2% y/y. The implication is not hike now, but hold longer: policymakers likely need clearer core-goods disinflation and evidence that the oil shock is not broadening before they can justify further cuts.

United States — NAHB / Wells Fargo Housing Market Index (Apr)
Release Date: 15 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 10:00 ET
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

NAHB / Wells Fargo HMI: 34 | Prior: 38 | Poll: 37 | Surprise: -3.0.

Professional Market Commentary

Official NAHB language and Reuters were aligned: builder sentiment fell sharply as spring demand failed to improve enough to offset elevated mortgage rates, rising material-cost uncertainty, and broader economic caution. The takeaway was not simply “builders cautious,” but “housing still deteriorating on both demand and cost fronts.”

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a genuine housing-quality deterioration print. Current sales conditions fell to 37, sales expectations fell to 42, traffic dropped to 22, and 36% of builders were still cutting prices while 60% used incentives; that combination says builders are not seeing a spring demand release valve. At the same time, NAHB said 62% of builders reported supplier cost increases due to higher fuel prices, which means the sector is facing demand weakness and input-cost pressure simultaneously rather than one clean cyclical problem.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior frozen USD housing sequence had already been weak and only intermittently stabilized by brief rate relief. April HMI reverses even that fragile stabilization and shows the builder side deteriorating alongside existing-home resale activity. The regime implication is that housing is broadening from a resale weakness story into a fuller sector drag.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This matters more than a typical sentiment survey because it validates the housing slowdown across both resale and new-build channels. JGM policy key: housing services inflation can still look “acceptable enough” on a lagged basis even while the real housing sector is weakening, so housing softness alone is not a sufficient trigger for easing. For policy, it strengthens the growth-downside case, but not enough by itself to clear the high bar for cuts while inflation data remain sticky.

For markets, the right use of this print is conditional. United States — Industrial Production / Capacity Utilization / Manufacturing Output (Mar)
Release Date: 16 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 09:15 ET
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Industrial Production MM: -0.5% | Prior: 0.2% | Revised: 0.7% | Poll: 0.1% | Surprise: -0.60%

Manufacturing Output MM: -0.1% | Prior: 0.2% | Revised: 0.4% | Poll: 0.1% | Surprise: -0.20%.

Professional Market Commentary

The official Fed data and Reuters framed this as a genuine hard-data setback after a stronger February base. Total industrial production fell 0.5%, manufacturing output slipped 0.1%, mining fell 1.2%, and utilities fell 2.3%; Reuters also highlighted a 3.7% drop in motor vehicle output, while total capacity utilization fell to 75.7%.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is weaker hard output, but not an across-the-board industrial collapse. The negative headline was amplified by utilities, mining, and autos, while manufacturing itself was only modestly down; still, that is a deterioration because February was revised up to a much stronger 0.7%, so March is falling from a firmer base rather than simply correcting a soft one.

JGM note: non-auto manufacturing still appears mildly positive, which argues against a severe interruption of underlying factory growth despite the energy shock. That leaves the best read as softer March hard activity, not a decisive factory recession, but it also means manufacturing no longer offers a clean offset to housing weakness because the sector’s underlying trend is only modestly positive while the headline is now vulnerable to energy and transport distortions.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior frozen USD week had left the industrial side in a modestly constructive position, with earlier production and manufacturing readings helping offset housing weakness. March reverses part of that progress by taking the headline back into contraction and softening manufacturing output off a stronger prior base. The regime implication is that hard activity no longer provides as clean a non-housing counterweight as it did earlier in the sequence.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For cross-asset mapping, this matters because it broadens the growth-softening story beyond housing while still leaving manufacturing softer rather than broken. That should discourage any simplistic “factory boom” narrative even after the strong Philly Fed survey; the next verification test is whether national manufacturing and capex-related releases validate the survey optimism or instead confirm that March marked a wider hard-data downshift.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: The price pipeline is still firm and housing plus hard activity are weakening, yet layoffs remain low and the strongest regional factory survey of the cycle just printed.

Why it matters: That combination keeps the Fed boxed in, because inflation is not cooling cleanly enough to ease while labor is not deteriorating fast enough to justify it.

Implication: Treat the week as a firmer stagflation-transmission sequence, but not yet a fully synchronized recessionary break. March PCE and national manufacturing are the next arbiters.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 6 Apr 2026 to 10 Apr 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

Relative to the prior frozen week, which had already ended in a clearer stagflationary bind driven by weaker forward growth, labor-freeze signals, and firmer input-cost pressure, the 6–10 Apr week hardened that regime rather than reversing it. The key change was not a clean collapse in activity, but the escalation of the inflation / expectations side of the bind: ISM services had already re-tightened the prices constraint early in the week, then headline CPI and Michigan inflation expectations pushed the shock more directly into households. At the same time, labor-leading indicators softened further and confidence deteriorated, but February hard activity, capex, wholesale, and consumption data still did not confirm a full demand break.

The resulting weekly regime is therefore tighter and more dangerous, but still internally split. Core and median inflation stayed relatively contained even as headline inflation, inflation expectations, and real-income damage worsened; claims still showed low fire even as hiring-demand indicators cooled; and backward-looking hard data kept the growth side from resolving into outright contraction. The Fed implication is a deeper hawkish-hold constraint than in the prior week: the economy still looks too firm in hard February data to justify easing, but inflation psychology and household purchasing-power compression have deteriorated enough to make comfort on inflation impossible.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week did not deliver a clean growth break. February hard data were still firmer than late-week surveys implied: real PCE rose 0.1% m/m, nominal consumption 0.5%, ex-transport durables 0.8%, nondefense capital goods ex-aircraft 0.6% and later 0.7% revised, wholesale sales 2.7%, inventories 0.8%, and factory orders were flat with ex-transport orders up 1.2%. But Q4 GDP final slipped to 0.5%, personal income fell 0.1%, ISM services activity slowed sharply, and confidence deteriorated. The correct read is fragile pre-shock momentum, not confirmed collapse.

Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
The labor bucket weakened further at the leading-indicator level. Employment Trends fell below the revised prior month, ISM services employment dropped to 45.2, and both PCSI and Michigan signaled softer household confidence. But claims did not validate labor breakage: initial claims rose only to 219k and continuing claims fell to 1.794 million. The labor regime is therefore cooler hiring appetite and softer labor demand, not outright unemployment stress.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
This was the dominant deterioration bucket. ISM prices paid surged to 70.7, headline CPI jumped 0.9% m/m and 3.3% y/y, energy drove most of the move, and Michigan 1-year inflation expectations rose to 4.8% with 5-year expectations at 3.4%. February core PCE also stayed stuck at 0.4% m/m. The mitigating point is composition: core CPI, median CPI, and Cleveland trimmed measures all held at 0.2%, while Dallas trimmed inflation remained a disinflationary counter-signal. So the week worsened the inflation problem mainly through headline transmission and expectations rather than a clean broad-core re-acceleration.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Conditions tightened through household affordability, sentiment, and real-income transmission rather than through a fresh market-rate spike. Mortgage rates eased modestly to 6.51%, but housing demand remained soft, purchase applications stayed below year-ago levels, real weekly earnings fell 0.9%, and consumer sentiment collapsed. The operative tightening channel was therefore effective purchasing-power compression and confidence deterioration, not relief.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The Fed constraint intensified. ISM prices, headline CPI, and Michigan expectations all moved in the wrong direction, while core and median inflation stayed contained enough to argue against a pure re-hiking interpretation. At the same time, claims and February hard activity remained too firm to justify a fast easing pivot. The policy mix is therefore a harder hawkish hold than last week: communications must stay inflation-focused, but the data still stop short of proving a broad-based underlying inflation re-acceleration or a full demand collapse.

C. FULL RELEASE ARCHIVE

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This block did not create a new policy signal, but it tightened the existing one. February PCE was too firm to reopen easing hope, while the income/saving mix and weak Q4 headline confirmed the economy entered the war shock already fragile. Strategically, this is best treated as the last clean pre-war baseline: March PCE later in April becomes the first decisive war-era inflation read.

United States — CPI / Core CPI / Real Weekly Earnings / CPI Wage Earner (Mar)
Release Date: 10 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Core CPI MM, SA: 0.2% | Prior: 0.2% | Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: -0.10%

Real Weekly Earnings MM: -0.9% | Prior: 0.1%
CPI Wage Earner: 323.500 | Prior: 319.422

Professional Market Commentary

The official BLS release showed a large headline shock but a softer core. Energy rose 10.9% in March, gasoline surged 21.2% and accounted for nearly three quarters of the monthly all-items increase, while core CPI rose only 0.2% and core y/y came in below consensus at 2.6%. Early market commentary focused on the politically alarming 3.3% headline, but the analytic split was clear: energy shock up front, core not yet re-accelerating broadly.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the week’s most important inflation block because it forces a distinction between headline shock and underlying trend. Headline CPI at 0.9% m/m and 3.3% y/y is a genuine escalation in consumer prices, but the composition says most of the jump came from gasoline and broader energy. Core at 0.2% m/m and median CPI at 0.2% both say the broad middle of the price distribution did not yet accelerate with the headline. The deeper macro problem is elsewhere: real weekly earnings fell 0.9%, so the energy shock is immediately compressing purchasing power even before core spillovers fully show up. State transition: escalation.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not a hawkish-reacceleration block in the pure core sense, but it is still bad for the Fed. The combination of energy-led headline shock and real-income squeeze keeps policy on hold and likely forces tougher inflation-risk communication. The strategist takeaway is not “Fed hikes next,” but “Fed cannot sound comfortable while expectations and household inflation pain are rising.”

Professional Market Commentary

This was the week’s other decisive block. But the vintage caveat is real: because almost all interviews were completed before the cease-fire announcement, the final April reading is likely to improve from this preliminary trough. State transition: escalation.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the Fed, this is a communications problem more than a mechanical policy trigger. Expectations are moving the wrong way even though the core inflation data are not yet clearly re-accelerating. That combination argues for a hawkish hold and for more explicit emphasis on anchoring expectations, even if the final Michigan read partially reverses.

United States — Cleveland Fed CPI (Mar)
Release Date: 10 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 17:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Cleveland Fed CPI: 0.2% | Prior: 0.2%

Professional Market Commentary

The Cleveland Fed median CPI page showed median CPI up 0.2% in March and the 16% trimmed-mean CPI also up 0.2%, both updated on April 10. The Fed’s own description emphasizes that these measures can provide a better signal of the underlying inflation trend by omitting outliers.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the cleanest counterweight to the week’s headline inflation narrative. Median CPI at 0.2% matches core CPI at 0.2% and says the broad interior of the price distribution did not accelerate with the headline. HF Take — Strategist Layer

Important analytically, less important tactically. It supports the case that the Fed should resist overreacting to one energy-dominated CPI print, but it does not remove the need for a hawkish tone because expectations and household purchasing-power damage both worsened at the same time.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Headline CPI and Michigan inflation expectations surged, but core CPI and median CPI both stayed at 0.2%, while February hard activity data remained firmer than the collapse implied by confidence surveys.
Why it matters: This is the split between a temporary energy/expectations shock and a true broad-based stagflation regime.
Implication: The Fed remains on hawkish hold, with communication pulled tighter by expectations even though the underlying core inflation pulse has not yet clearly re-accelerated.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

CPI / Core CPI / Real Weekly Earnings (10 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This was the week’s decisive inflation block. Headline CPI delivered the first clear official energy-shock surge, but core and median CPI stayed relatively contained, while real weekly earnings fell 0.9%. That combination matters more than the headline alone because it says the immediate macro damage is flowing through household purchasing power as much as through broad core inflation.
U Mich Sentiment / Inflation Expectations (10 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: The 47.6 preliminary sentiment reading and 4.8% one-year inflation expectations created the week’s biggest Fed communications problem. Even allowing for the pre-cease-fire survey timing, the breadth of the deterioration and the rise in long-run expectations make this the cleanest evidence that the shock is reaching households psychologically as well as financially.
ISM N-Mfg PMI / Business Activity / Employment / New Orders / Prices Paid (6 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This was the release that set the week’s macro frame before the hard data arrived. It showed a services sector still expanding, but with worsening employment and cost pressure, and it correctly signaled the week’s central theme: demand had not yet broken, but the inflation constraint had tightened materially.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 30 Mar 2026 to 3 Apr 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week ended in a clearer stagflationary bind rather than either clean resilience or clean recession. Backward-looking activity data still showed pockets of support — retail sales beat, payrolls beat on the headline, and manufacturing remained in expansion — but the more important forward stack deteriorated materially: Dallas services weakened sharply, S&P Global services fell into contraction, JOLTS confirmed a low-hire / low-fire labor freeze, housing financing tightened further, and consumer expectations stayed recessionary. At the same time, cost pressure intensified meaningfully through ISM Prices Paid and firmer survey pricing. The resulting regime was not outright collapse, but a more binding mix of softer forward growth, weaker service-sector quality, and rising input-cost pressure that kept the Fed constrained.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Retail sales rebounded strongly across all major aggregates, ISM manufacturing improved to 52.7, and trade was better than feared for Q1 arithmetic. But those offsets were increasingly backward-looking or compositionally flattered. Dallas services deteriorated sharply, S&P Global services fell into contraction at 49.8, housing price momentum was already soft before mortgage rates rose, and business inventories still pointed to a cautious corporate backdrop. The week therefore showed residual Q1 resilience but a weaker Q2 forward growth setup.

Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
JOLTS was the clearest underlying labor signal: openings fell, hires and quits moved to multi-year lows, and the labor market increasingly looked frozen rather than healthy. Claims stayed low and payrolls beat on the headline, but payroll strength was qualified by narrow composition, softer household-survey quality, lower participation, higher U6, and a large downward revision to February. ADP also showed weak breadth and sticky wages. The net weekly message was not labor breakage, but a deteriorating labor-quality / labor-flow regime.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
The decisive signal came from ISM Prices Paid at 78.3, a sharp jump that moved the cost shock into live data. Dallas manufacturing and S&P Global services both preserved a high-cost, weaker-growth mix rather than any clean disinflationary trend, while consumer confidence internals showed stronger inflation anxiety. Softer average earnings helped at the margin, but the week’s inflation signal was still one of worsening input pressure rather than easing price tension.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
MBA mortgage rates rose to 6.57%, tightening financing conditions further into an already fragile housing sector. FHFA and Case-Shiller both showed weak price momentum even before the latest rate shock, implying greater Q2 sensitivity. More broadly, the week’s macro mix — firmer cost pressure, weaker forward services data, and no decisive labor break — reinforced a restrictive financial-conditions backdrop rather than relief.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The week removed any clean easing narrative. Payrolls were too firm on the headline, unemployment edged down to 4.3%, retail sales still showed Q1 demand support, and ISM Prices Paid materially tightened the inflation side of the bind. At the same time, the forward growth stack worsened through services contraction, weaker labor flows and softer confidence. That combination left the Fed more constrained, not less: growth was not weak enough to justify easing, while cost pressure was too firm to relax the inflation guardrail.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was a textbook false-dawn release. The surface read was undeniably strong: all major aggregates beat, the control group printed well, and the composition showed a broad-based rebound. But the timing and drivers mattered more than the headline. This was a backward-looking Q1 resilience print supported by weather normalization, autos, clothing and tax refunds, not proof that demand would remain durable into Q2. The more relevant forward setup was worse: higher fuel costs, more regressive real-income pressure and fading temporary supports. The right hierarchy was strong nominal Q1 spending, but weaker forward real consumption.
State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file:
The consumer had already been described as resilient but fragile. Retail sales confirmed the resilience, but only in backward-looking data and under temporary supports.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This gave the Fed cover to stay restrictive because Q1 demand still looked solid. But the asymmetry was unfavorable: markets that extrapolated the strength forward risked misreading a print whose main value was to mark the peak of the bounce rather than the start of a new uptrend.

UNITED STATES — ISM Manufacturing PMI / ISM Mfg Prices Paid / ISM Manuf Employment Idx / ISM Manuf New Orders Idx (Mar)
Release Date: 1 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 16:00
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

ISM Manufacturing PMI: 52.7 | Prior: 52.4 | Poll: 52.5 | Surprise: 0.20
ISM Mfg Prices Paid: 78.3 | Prior: 70.5 | Poll: 73.0 | Surprise: 5.30
ISM Manuf Employment Idx: 48.7 | Prior: 48.8
ISM Manuf New Orders Idx: 53.5 | Prior: 55.8

Professional Market Commentary

The release split the narrative immediately between “growth improving” and “inflation problem worsening.” The growth side focused on the strongest headline PMI since 2022 and firmer production. The more consequential tape focused on Prices Paid at 78.3, broad-based cost increases across industries, and explicit references to tariffs, metals and conflict-driven petroleum inputs as cost drivers.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was the defining macro release of the week. The growth side of the report was respectable: headline PMI improved, production was solid and new orders remained expansionary. But the inflation side mattered more. Prices Paid surged to its highest level since 2022, supplier deliveries slowed further, and employment remained in contraction. That is not a clean demand-overheating signal. It is a supply-cost shock embedded inside still-positive activity. The internal mix therefore tightened the policy bind materially: activity was not weak enough to justify easing, but costs were worsening fast enough to make easing even harder.
State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
The U.S. had already been in a growth / inflation tension regime. ISM materially tightened that tension by moving the cost shock from risk scenario into live data.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The market risk was to overweight the headline PMI and underweight Prices Paid. Strategically, Prices Paid was the regime signal: it locked the Fed into a more constrained stance and kept inflation risk at the center of the week.

THURSDAY 2 APR 2026

UNITED STATES — Challenger Layoffs (Mar)
Release Date: 2 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 13:30
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Challenger Layoffs: 60.620k | Prior: 48.307k

Professional Market Commentary

Headline coverage leaned on the benign aggregate Q1 framing, but the richer tape focused on the changing reasons and sectors for announced cuts. The most important theme was the accelerating share of layoffs explicitly attributed to AI, especially in technology.

Comment — Economist Layer

The aggregate number was not alarming in a cyclical sense, but the structure underneath was noteworthy. Technology remained the largest source of layoffs, and AI rose to the leading stated reason for March cuts. That did not yet create a broad labor-market quantity problem, but it did speak to deteriorating labor-market quality and structural pressure on mid-skill and white-collar roles. In that sense, Challenger was more relevant as a composition signal for labor-market evolution than as a near-term recession warning.
State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
Labor had already been weakening through slower flows rather than mass layoffs. Challenger fit that pattern: no broad firing wave, but growing evidence of restructuring and structural labor displacement.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Not a dominant cyclical signal, but relevant to the labor-quality debate. It argued for caution against reading later headline payroll resilience as evidence that the labor market remained broadly healthy underneath.
