FRIDAY 3 APR 2026

UNITED STATES — Non-Farm Payrolls / Private Payrolls / Manufacturing Payrolls / Government Payrolls / Unemployment Rate / Average Earnings MM / Average Earnings YY / Average Workweek Hrs / Labor Force Partic / U6 Underemployment (Mar)
Release Date: 3 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Non-Farm Payrolls: 178k | Prior: -92k | Revised: -133k | Poll: 60k | Surprise: 118.0k
Private Payrolls: 186k | Prior: -86k | Revised: -129k | Poll: 70k | Surprise: 116.0k
Manufacturing Payrolls: 15k | Prior: -12k | Revised: -6k | Poll: -5k | Surprise: 20.0k
Government Payrolls: -8k | Prior: -6k | Revised: -4k
Unemployment Rate: 4.3% | Prior: 4.4% | Poll: 4.4% | Surprise: -0.10%
Average Earnings MM: 0.2% | Prior: 0.4% | Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: -0.10
Average Earnings YY: 3.5% | Prior: 3.8% | Poll: 3.7% | Surprise: -0.20
Average Workweek Hrs: 34.2 | Prior: 34.3 | Poll: 34.3 | Surprise: -0.10
Labor Force Partic: 61.9% | Prior: 62.0%
U6 Underemployment: 8.0% | Prior: 7.9%

Professional Market Commentary

Headline coverage leaned heavily on the payroll beat and lower unemployment rate, especially in thin Good Friday conditions. The richer tape was more conditional: the beat was flattered by healthcare strike reversal and weather normalization, while wages softened, the household survey looked weaker than the establishment survey, and February was revised sharply lower.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was a structurally mixed labor report with a misleadingly strong headline. The establishment survey beat was real, but the composition mattered. Healthcare contributed heavily and included a large mechanical strike-reversal effect, construction benefited from weather rebound, and manufacturing improved versus expectations. That made the headline too strong to read as the new underlying trend. The wage side was more dovish: both monthly and annual average earnings missed, and annual wage growth fell to 3.5%, its lowest pace since 2021. Meanwhile the household survey sent a softer message: participation fell, U6 rose, and broader indicators of labor quality remained weak. February’s downward revision to -133k also mattered. The right synthesis was not “labor bounced back cleanly,” but “temporary supports produced a stronger March headline on top of an underlying slow-hire, low-fire market with weaker quality.”
State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file:
The earlier week had already established labor as a freeze regime rather than a firing regime. Payrolls did not break that interpretation. They delayed a sharper deterioration signal, but did not overturn the weaker-flow evidence from JOLTS or the softer household-survey quality metrics.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the Fed, this was awkward but not liberating. The headline beat and 4.3% unemployment removed any immediate easing cover, but softer wages and weaker labor quality kept the growth side from looking truly robust. Markets that treated the report as an uncomplicated labor reacceleration risked misreading a print flattered by one-offs and weaker underneath than the headline suggested.

UNITED STATES — S&P Global Comp PMI Final / S&P Global Svcs PMI Final (Mar)
Release Date: 3 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 15:45
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

S&P Global Comp PMI Final: 50.3 | Prior: 51.4
S&P Global Svcs PMI Final: 49.8 | Prior: 51.1

Professional Market Commentary

This release was dramatically under-covered because it landed on the same day as payrolls in thin holiday trading. The more careful tape, however, treated it as one of the most important forward-looking signals of the week: services slipped into contraction for the first time since early 2023, the composite dropped toward stall speed, and pricing pressures intensified.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was arguably the most important forward-looking state transition of the week. Services at 49.8 ended a 37-month expansion streak, and the move from flash to final showed that conditions worsened materially in the second half of March. New orders softened, export demand deteriorated further, employment slipped, and input and output prices accelerated. The release aligned strikingly well with Dallas services, weaker consumer expectations and labor-freeze signals. It also sat in direct tension with the stronger payroll headline: payrolls were backward-looking and compositionally flattered, while services PMI pointed to a live deterioration in the largest part of the economy. The most accurate read was that the U.S. service economy was now buckling under the combined weight of higher costs and weaker confidence, even if the labor data had not fully caught up yet.
State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior tape had already been moving toward slower domestic demand, but this was the first clean national services contraction signal. It validated the earlier Dallas services weakness and materially strengthened the case that the U.S. entered Q2 with a deteriorating service-sector backdrop.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This was the release most likely to matter for the next phase of the macro narrative even though it received less coverage than payrolls. Strategically, it sharpened the stagflationary bind: softer services growth, worsening confidence, firmer prices. It also suggested that forward activity risk was more negative than the headline labor report implied.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Backward-looking U.S. activity data still printed resilient, but the forward-looking services, labor-flow and confidence indicators deteriorated materially while input-cost inflation accelerated.

Why it matters: This blocks both a clean soft-landing narrative and a clean easing narrative.

Implication: The U.S. enters the next window in an active stagflationary bind — softer forward growth, firmer cost pressure, and a Fed that remains constrained.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

ISM Manufacturing PMI / Prices Paid (1 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This was the policy-defining release of the week. The headline PMI showed activity still expanding, but Prices Paid at 78.3 moved the inflation shock into live data and tightened the Fed constraint materially.
S&P Global Svcs PMI Final / Comp PMI Final (3 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This was the most important forward-looking growth signal of the week. Services fell into contraction, the composite moved toward stall speed, and the release validated earlier service-sector weakness that headline labor data did not capture.
JOLTS Job Openings (31 Mar 2026)
Why it matters: This established the labor market’s underlying structure. Hires and quits at multi-year lows confirmed a slow-hire, low-fire freeze that later claims and payrolls did not overturn.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 23 Mar 2026 to 27 Mar 2026
A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS
The week resolved into a clearer inflation-constrained, stagflation-risk configuration rather than a growth-break regime. Growth data stayed mixed: private construction spending and mortgage-finance transmission deteriorated, national services momentum softened, and consumer psychology worsened, but regional manufacturing surveys stabilized or improved and the external balance improved. Labor did not crack in hard data — claims stayed low and continuing claims fell — but softer employment signals in CFNAI and the flash PMI showed some cooling at the margin.
The decisive weekly shift came from the inflation and expectations side. Q4 unit labor costs revised higher, import and export prices broadened sharply even before the full March war shock, PMI price gauges accelerated, and Michigan showed a worsening near-term inflation scare with only conditionally anchored long-run expectations. The resulting regime is not hard-data recession confirmation; it is restrictive-hold reinforcement under a worse growth/inflation mix.
B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD
Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week’s growth signal stayed mixed rather than broken. Construction spending missed at -0.3% m/m with clear private and residential weakness, and higher mortgage rates plus falling applications reinforced tighter financing transmission into housing. But regional factory data improved — Richmond stabilized and KC Fed strengthened materially — while the current-account deficit narrowed sharply. National activity softened, yet the aggregate still reads as below-trend growth with offsetting pockets rather than outright contraction.
Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Hard labor data remained resilient enough to block a clean deterioration call. Initial claims held at 210k in line with consensus, the four-week average edged lower, and continuing claims fell to 1.819 million, preserving the low-fire regime. Against that, CFNAI weakness was partly employment-led and the flash PMI showed employment falling for the first time in over a year. The week therefore pointed to cooling labor demand, not a labor-market break.
Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
This was the clearest negative bucket. Unit labor costs were revised up to 4.4% against 3.5% consensus, import prices rose 1.3% m/m and export prices 1.5% m/m with broader nonfuel and capital-goods strength, and the PMI block combined slower activity with sharper price acceleration. Michigan then showed one-year inflation expectations jumping to 3.8%. The inflation impulse broadened rather than faded.
Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Conditions tightened through the rates-to-housing channel. MBA 30-year mortgage rates rose to 6.43%, the highest since October, while total applications fell 10.5%, refinances 14.6%, and purchases 5.4%. Construction spending weakness in private and residential segments reinforced that financing-sensitive domestic demand remained under pressure. The week’s market transmission ran in the restrictive direction.
Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The week strengthened restrictive-hold logic rather than opening an easing path. Hard labor data were still too stable to force a dovish reinterpretation, while imported-price pressure, higher labor-cost revisions, hotter PMI prices, and worsening near-term household inflation expectations all intensified the inflation side of the trade-off. The final weekly mix left the Fed facing softer growth signals without the disinflation relief needed to ease the constraint.

State transition: continuation of the low-fire regime.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD labor sequence already leaned toward cooling labor, not collapse. Claims preserve that classification and argue against an imminent hard-data deterioration on the firing side.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
This keeps the week’s stagflation read centered on prices and confidence rather than on a cracked labor market. That is important for the Fed, because softer growth is not yet arriving through the one channel that would clearly force a dovish reinterpretation.

Professional Market Commentary
This block improves materially with the new scout because the 5-year field can no longer be read in isolation. The official Michigan release showed sentiment at 53.3, one-year inflation expectations at 3.8%, and five-year expectations at 3.2%, while Reuters and Joanne Hsu’s statement made clear that the shock was transmitting through gasoline prices, financial-market volatility, and post-conflict interviews. The better tape summary is: near-term inflation fear worsened sharply, while long-run expectations remained only conditionally anchored.
Comment — Economist Layer
The scout’s most useful correction is that a stable 5-year figure can create a falsely neutral impression. The full release was materially worse: sentiment was revised down sharply from the preliminary reading, one-year inflation expectations jumped to 3.8%, and the official note said interviews completed after February 28 showed higher inflation expectations than earlier interviews. So the right read is deterioration in the full sentiment complex with only conditional long-run anchoring. State transition: deterioration.
Context from prior regional live file:
The earlier USD file had already shown fragile consumer psychology alongside still-functioning demand. Final March Michigan worsens the psychology side materially and aligns it more closely with the week’s inflation shock.

This is not panic-level Fed evidence because the 5-year measure did not break higher, but it clearly worsens the near-term trade-off. Hsu’s own framing makes the anchoring story conditional on the conflict not becoming protracted and energy prices not passing through more broadly, which is exactly the falsification trigger the Fed now has to watch.
SIGNAL TENSION CHECK
Main contradiction: Hard labor and regional factory data held up, but the inflation and household-expectations layers worsened sharply through import prices, mortgage-rate transmission, and Michigan’s one-year expectations jump, while five-year expectations only stayed conditionally anchored.
Why it matters: That blocks a simple “softer growth means easier Fed” interpretation.
Implication: The week’s dominant impulse is restrictive-hold reinforcement via stagflation-risk repricing, not a hard-data recession turn.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 16 Mar 2026 to 19 Mar 2026
A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS
US weekly macro regime hardened into inflation-constrained resilience with a housing contraction pocket. Manufacturing and goods-flow data held up: industrial production and manufacturing output beat, Philly decisively resolved Empire noise in favor of continued expansion, wholesale sales beat while inventories fell, and claims preserved the low-firing / slow-hiring equilibrium. But housing deteriorated materially: NAHB only stabilized from a weak base, pending sales looked like a rate-window false dawn, mortgage rates re-tightened, permits stayed weak, and new home sales collapsed.
Inflation re-heated rather than eased. February PPI showed broader pipeline and services pressure, and the FOMC responded with a hold but a tighter easing constraint rather than a renewed normalization bridge. The week therefore ended with growth still positive outside housing, labor not weak enough to offset inflation, and the Fed stuck in hold-with-tight-constraint mode.
B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD
Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Industrial Production rose 0.2% m/m, manufacturing output rose 0.2% m/m with a stronger prior revision, and Philly surged to 18.1 with stronger shipments, employment, and capex intentions. Wholesale sales rose 0.5% m/m while inventories fell 0.5%, reinforcing a still-firm goods-flow backdrop. But housing moved the other way: permits remained weak, pending sales looked backward-looking, and new home sales fell to 0.587M with a -17.6% m/m drop. The weekly growth signal is therefore not recessionary, but clearly split between resilient non-housing activity and contracting rate-sensitive demand.
Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Initial claims fell to 205k against a 215k consensus and the 4-week average edged lower, keeping the low-firing equilibrium intact. Philly employment returned positive, adding to the message that manufacturing labor conditions did not roll over. But continuing claims rose to 1.857M and the broader file repeatedly framed labor as low firing rather than strong hiring. The week therefore preserved labor stability, not renewed labor tightness.
Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
PPI Final Demand rose 0.7% m/m and 3.4% y/y, core ex-food/energy rose 0.5% m/m and ex-food/energy/trade also rose 0.5% m/m, with services and goods both contributing. The file’s economist-layer conclusion was that the disinflation stall broadened into renewed pipeline pressure rather than remained contained. Monday’s softer Empire price cue was overwhelmed by Wednesday’s much hotter producer-price sequence. Inflation was the clearest adverse macro development of the week.
Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
MBA 30-year mortgage rates rose to 6.30% from 6.19%, explicitly reversing the February affordability window that had helped pending sales. Housing commentary throughout the file tied this to renewed oil / inflation risk and a re-tightening housing-rate channel. The FOMC event also came with higher-yield / weaker-equity market interpretation, and the week’s housing stack showed tighter financing conditions biting directly. Financial conditions therefore tightened in the sectors that matter most for rate sensitivity.
Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The Fed held at 3.50-3.75%, but the March SEP raised inflation projections while leaving the nominal rate path unchanged, producing an “unchanged dots, hawkier constraint” outcome. Powell described activity as solid, consumption resilient, investment expanding, housing weak, and inflation risks vulnerable to higher energy prices. After the hot PPI print and strong Philly follow-through, the bar for cuts rose rather than fell. The easing cycle remains possible later, but no longer operates as the default organizing narrative.

MONDAY 16 MAR 2026
UNITED STATES — NY Fed Manufacturing (Mar)
Release Date: 16 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:30
Importance: **
Reuters Data
NY Fed Manufacturing: -0.20 | Prior: 7.10 | Poll: 3.90 | Surprise: -4.100
Professional Market Commentary
Institutional framing was softer than expected on the headline but less bearish on the internals. Officially, business activity was little changed rather than collapsing: new orders increased modestly, shipments declined, unfilled orders rose, delivery times lengthened, supply availability worsened slightly, employment rose modestly, and firms remained optimistic while capital spending plans strengthened. The key tape emphasis was that the miss was heavily shipment-led, while the most Fed-relevant internal shift was a sharp deceleration in input-price pressure.
Comment — Economist Layer
The headline miss is real, but the composition does not validate a clean demand-break narrative. The important distinction is orders versus execution: new orders stayed positive while shipments turned negative, with delivery times lengthening and supply availability worsening. That mix is more consistent with friction, timing, or logistics noise than with broad factory demand destruction. Prices paid falling sharply is the other key signal: Monday’s first manufacturing print leaned softer on inflation pressure than the headline alone implied.
State transition: mild deterioration in survey tone, but not a demand-led break.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD sequence had already migrated toward a modest manufacturing stabilization story: ISM manufacturing had improved, January industrial production had strengthened, and the February Philly Fed survey had been firmer, though the later January durables/core-capex release weakened the business-investment confirmation. Against that backdrop, March Empire softens the short-cycle survey tone but does not overturn the broader “soft landing with friction” manufacturing regime.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
For policy, this is only marginally dovish. A weaker regional survey is too small on its own to move the Fed, but softer input-price optics slightly reduce the risk that manufacturing becomes a new inflation problem. The right read was verification mode rather than extrapolation mode, with Philly Fed later in the week the cleaner test of whether Empire was early warning or just another noisy regional survey.

So the report supported activity, but not overheating.

HF Take — Strategist Layer
This was modestly growth-supportive and only weakly hawkish. For the Fed reaction function, stronger output mattered less than the fact that spare industrial capacity remained meaningful. Combined with the softer Empire price signal, Monday’s industrial mix still looked like activity holding up without a parallel re-heating in supply-side inflation pressure.

Professional Market Commentary
Institutional and official commentary treated the print as stabilization, not recovery. But the narrative stayed dominated by affordability constraints, high land/labor/construction costs, persistent use of incentives, and buyer hesitation around rates and uncertainty.

The right read was better breadth from a weak base. But nothing here justified a housing-recovery call. At 38, sentiment remained deeply below neutral, while price cuts and incentives showed demand was still functioning only with support. The additional macro nuance was that geopolitics had clearly entered housing commentary via mortgage-rate and oil-price uncertainty.
State transition: stabilization from a low base, without recovery.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior housing sequence had already moved toward “weak pocket stabilizing” rather than outright deterioration. February NAHB was soft, while the subsequent January starts-permits split showed better current construction activity but a weaker forward pipeline. March HMI nudged that sequence a bit firmer on sentiment, but not enough to turn housing into a new growth engine.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
Housing remained one of the cleanest transmission channels for the Fed, but this release did not force a policy conclusion. It said lower mortgage rates help at the margin, yet demand still stalls below a true recovery threshold and remains vulnerable to geopolitical-rate spillovers. For cross-asset interpretation, that was mildly supportive for the growth floor but still too fragile for an aggressive cyclical housing re-rating.

February captured a short affordability window when mortgage rates were lower; the bounce therefore came off an extremely depressed base rather than from a durable housing turn. The regional split reinforces that: the Midwest led, consistent with affordability sensitivity, while the Northeast remained the structural weak point. The most important Claude contribution here is the “false dawn” distinction: the data improved, but the conditions that enabled the improvement were already reversing by release date.

Pending sales fit that same bucket: better than feared, but still operating at historically suppressed levels and still highly dependent on the rate channel.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
For policy, this confirms housing remains rate-sensitive enough to respond quickly to even modest financing relief, but not strong enough to absorb a renewed rate back-up. The key point is transmission: February lower rates helped; March higher rates threaten to undo it. That makes housing a downside-growth channel if inflation keeps long rates and mortgage rates elevated after the PPI/FOMC sequence.

UNITED STATES — PPI Final Demand (Feb)
Release Date: 18 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:30
Importance: ****
Reuters Data
PPI Machine Manuf'ing: 195.9 | Prior: 195.4 | Revised: 195.3
PPI Final Demand YY: 3.4% | Prior: 2.9% | Poll: 2.9% | Surprise: 0.50%
PPI Final Demand MM: 0.7% | Prior: 0.5% | Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: 0.40%
PPI exFood/Energy YY: 3.9% | Prior: 3.6% | Revised: 3.5% | Poll: 3.7% | Surprise: 0.20%
PPI exFood/Energy MM: 0.5% | Prior: 0.8% | Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: 0.20%
PPI ex Food/Energy/Tr YY: 3.5% | Prior: 3.4%
PPI ex Food/Energy/Tr MM: 0.5% | Prior: 0.3% | Revised: 0.4%
Professional Market Commentary
Claude’s first pass was strong and survives verification: this was a hawkish inflation print, not a nuanced softening story. Officially, more than half of the February rise came from services, final demand goods rose 1.1%, and final demand less foods, energy, and trade services rose 0.5% for a tenth consecutive monthly increase. Reuters also emphasized that services and a range of goods drove the strongest monthly increase in seven months, while market pricing pushed the next expected Fed cut out toward late 2026 or early 2027.
Comment — Economist Layer
The core signal is tighter than the headline alone. First, the sequential pattern matters: headline PPI accelerated again, extending the three-month re-heating sequence. Second, the composition is double-sided in a bad way for the Fed: goods were boosted by food and energy, which can be partially dismissed as exogenous or volatile, but services also rose 0.5%, and the core ex-food/energy/trade measure advanced 0.5% for a tenth straight monthly increase. Third, Claude is right to distinguish volatile from sticky service components: traveler accommodation is noisy, but financial and other core service inputs are more relevant to the PCE pass-through. So the economist-layer conclusion is not merely “hot print,” but “pipeline pressure plus sticky services means the disinflation stall is broadening.”
State transition: deterioration / reversal.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD inflation regime was “plateau with softer composition”: February CPI had avoided re-acceleration but had not solved the inflation problem, and January core PCE at 3.1% had kept the sticky floor intact. Wednesday’s PPI weakens that prior equilibrium materially. Instead of softening pipeline pressure, it suggests inflation re-acceleration risk was building before the Fed even spoke.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
This is the week’s decisive inflation gate. It does not mechanically force a hike, but it materially narrows the Fed’s easing optionality and shifts the burden of proof back onto future CPI/PCE disinflation. The Claude tape’s “services stickiness is the binding constraint” framing is the right one for Section 4.

Claude’s first pass broadly holds after verification: the formal decision was a hold, but the full event read slightly more hawkish than the unchanged median one-cut path alone suggested. The key analytical point is that the Fed did not formally abandon normalization, but it did tighten the policy constraint around it. Powell’s opening statement explicitly described activity as solid, consumer spending as resilient, business fixed investment as expanding, housing as weak, and near-term inflation risk as vulnerable to higher energy prices. The SEP left the nominal rate path unchanged, yet raised inflation forecasts and kept unemployment only modestly higher. That is why the economist-layer read is “unchanged dots, hawkier constraint.” Claude’s framing that this is a suspension of the easing narrative rather than a formal shift to hikes is the correct synthesis.
State transition: reversal in policy communication toward hold-with-tighter-constraint.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior USD sequence ended with labor cooling but not breaking, inflation plateauing rather than decisively easing, and growth still resilient but uneven. The question entering this meeting was whether the Fed would keep patience as a normalization bridge or start leaning harder against inflation risk. After the PPI print and the March communication, the answer is the latter: the Fed is still on hold, but the bar for resuming cuts is now higher than it looked earlier in the week.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
This is the dominant policy event of the week. The reaction function is now clearer: the Fed can still cut later, but only if inflation resumes credible downward progress and the growth/labor side softens enough to offset energy and tariff risks. In strategic terms, the easing cycle is not dead, but it is no longer the default organizing narrative.

UNITED STATES — Philly Fed Business Indx (Mar)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:30
Importance: ***
Reuters Data
Philly Fed Business Indx: 18.1 | Prior: 16.3 | Poll: 10.0 | Surprise: 8.10
Professional Market Commentary
This was a major upside surprise and the decisive verification test for Monday’s Empire ambiguity. Officially, current activity rose for a third consecutive month, shipments surged, employment returned to positive territory, and future employment and capex intentions improved materially. The dominant professional read was that Empire’s soft print was regional noise rather than a signal of national manufacturing deterioration.
Comment — Economist Layer
The biggest analytical contribution here is resolution, not just strength. Monday’s Empire miss had left open the possibility of a wider March manufacturing slowdown. Philly shuts that down. The shipments surge is the most important internal component because it shows actual output flow rather than just sentiment. Employment and hours also improved, which reinforces the “hard activity holding” story. The main caveat is the price side: prices paid re-accelerated and uncertainty as a constraint on capacity utilization rose sharply in the special questions. So this is not a benign growth print; it is a strong manufacturing print with embedded fragility and persistent cost pressure.
State transition: improvement / expansion, with uncertainty as embedded fragility.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior manufacturing sequence was mixed: Empire soft, IP solid, capex signals constructive, and earlier survey improvement not yet fully confirmed. Philly now resolves that sequence in favor of continued expansion. The broader manufacturing bucket is stronger than Monday alone suggested, though still operating in an environment of elevated uncertainty and renewed producer-price pressure.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
This materially weakens the case for a growth-led dovish pivot. If manufacturing surveys had rolled over after the hot PPI and hawkishly constrained FOMC, the Fed would have faced more obvious two-sided pressure. Instead, Philly says activity is still holding, which leaves the policy constraint tighter, not looser.

UNITED STATES — New Home Sales-Units / New Home Sales Chg MM (Jan)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 15:00

Professional Market Commentary
This was the week’s most alarming housing release. Professional commentary acknowledged weather effects but did not treat them as a full explanation. Officially, new home sales fell to 587k, the lowest level since late 2022, while months’ supply jumped and median prices declined. The dominant tape read was that January housing demand was materially weaker than expected and that the March rate reversal made the forward outlook worse, not better.
Comment — Economist Layer
The data is substantially worse than a simple weather story. The magnitude of the miss, the downward revision to the December base, the broad regional weakness, and the rise in months’ supply together argue for genuine demand impairment. The price decline matters as well: this is no longer just a transaction slowdown but an active price-adjustment process. Read across the week with NAHB, pending sales, and MBA rates, the housing sector is not merely soft — it is contracting. Pending sales had looked like a false dawn; new home sales confirm the downside state.
State transition: deterioration into active sector contraction.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior housing narrative had already become one of weak stabilization rather than recovery. Thursday’s new home sales block breaks that softer reading. The sector is now showing simultaneous demand weakness, inventory overhang, and price correction, which is a materially worse configuration than earlier in the week.
HF Take — Strategist Layer
This is the strongest growth-dovish release of the week, but it arrives inside a macro mix where the Fed still cannot ease easily because inflation and manufacturing activity remain too firm. That tension is now explicit: housing is contracting, but the rest of the economy is not weakening enough to dominate the reaction function.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK
Main contradiction: The U.S. economy is still producing enough activity and enough inflation to keep the Fed constrained, while housing is deteriorating hard enough to argue against any tightening instinct. Manufacturing expanded, claims held stable, and wholesale demand was decent, but new home sales collapsed and the broader housing stack worsened materially.

Implication: The Fed remains stuck in hold-with-tight-constraint mode. Future inflation prints still determine easing optionality, but housing is becoming the clearest place where tighter financial conditions are biting.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 9 Mar 2026 to 13 Mar 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

US growth held up through the week but remained uneven rather than re-accelerating. Income, nominal spending, trade, and parts of housing kept the activity floor intact, while durable goods, core capex, and the Q4 GDP revision showed that business investment and the backward-looking growth profile were softer than the soft-landing headline suggests. Housing improved from weak levels but stayed split internally, with existing sales and starts stabilizing while permits kept the forward pipeline cautious.

Labor cooled without confirming a break: claims stayed low, layoffs did not accelerate, and JOLTS pushed back against a clean bearish labor extrapolation, even if softer hiring momentum remained visible in the broader sequence. Inflation was somewhat softer compositionally in CPI but not resolved in regime terms, with core PCE still firm enough to keep the Fed constrained. The weekly macro regime therefore remains soft landing with friction: demand holding, labor softening, inflation plateau.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Personal income rose 0.4% m/m, nominal consumption held at 0.4% m/m, and trade improved sharply on stronger exports, all of which supported the near-term activity floor. Existing home sales and housing starts argued for stabilization in parts of housing, but permits fell materially, durable goods missed badly, core capex was flat, and Q4 GDP was revised down to 0.7% with softer final sales. The weekly growth message was resilience with friction, not broadening acceleration.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Employment Trends improved only modestly and stayed low-signal, but the higher-value labor read came from claims and JOLTS. Initial claims at 213k, a lower four-week average, and softer continued claims argued against layoffs acceleration, while JOLTS openings rebounded above both prior and consensus. Labor is cooling, but the week did not validate a clean labor-break narrative.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
February CPI avoided re-acceleration and looked softer under the surface, with contained goods and some shelter moderation, but services stickiness remained present. January core PCE held at 0.4% m/m and rose to 3.1% y/y, confirming that disinflation is incomplete and that services remain the sticky core. The week therefore reinforced inflation plateau rather than delivering a decisive disinflation break.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
There was no decisive economy-wide easing or tightening shift during the week, but financing conditions remained restrictive enough to matter at the margin. The MBA 30-year mortgage rate rose to 6.19%, tempering the housing stabilization signal, while still-soft confidence readings showed households and firms remain uneasy. Conditions are restrictive enough to preserve friction, but not worsening sharply enough to change regime classification.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The week did not free the Fed from the inflation constraint. CPI helped by avoiding a new scare, but core PCE remained firm, and the GDP revision combined softer real growth with a firmer price backdrop. With demand still holding and labor not clearly breaking, the Fed remains in a data-dependent hold / wait-for-confirmation regime rather than moving toward aggressive easing repricing.

TUESDAY 10 MAR 2026

UNITED STATES — NFIB Business Optimism Index / Small Business Economic Trends (Feb)
Release Date: 10 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 11:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

NFIB Business Optimism Index: 98.8 | Prior: 99.3
Uncertainty Index: 88 | Prior: 91

Professional Market Commentary

No Tier 1 institutional debate detected.

Official NFIB framing was that optimism slipped slightly for a second straight month but remained just above its 52-year average, while uncertainty fell more meaningfully. The official emphasis was stronger recent sales and profit trends, lower uncertainty around capital expenditures, and still-tight labor conditions, offset by softer forward sales expectations.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a mixed small-business print, not a recession print. The current-state side improved in several places: actual nominal sales rose to a net 1%, earnings trends improved 7 points to net -14%, and the Employment Index rose to 103.5 with 33% of firms still reporting unfilled openings. But the forward impulse softened: expected real sales fell 8 points to net 8%, plans to create jobs fell 4 points to net 12%, and capex intentions held at a historically weak 18%. On inflation, the signal is two-handed: actual and planned selling-price increases both slowed, but compensation raising rose to a net 34%, the highest since March 2025. State transition: continuation of soft landing with friction.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior USD live sequence had already described the U.S. as a soft-landing-with-friction economy: demand holding, labor cooling but not breaking, and inflation plateauing rather than resolving. NFIB fits that regime closely. It pushes against a hard growth-break call because optimism stayed near average and actual sales improved, but it also pushes against a re-acceleration story because hiring plans, sales expectations, and capex intentions all remained soft.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the Fed, this is not a clean dovish small-business survey. Cooling price plans are mildly helpful, but persistent labor tightness and firmer compensation keep the release from delivering clear disinflation relief. Strategically, it reinforces a “growth not broken, but forward impulse softer” regime, which is more consistent with restrictive hold than with imminent easing.

DATA WINDOW: 2 Mar 2026 to 6 Mar 2026
A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS
The week produced a soft-landing versus early-labor-slowdown tension. Activity indicators were still expansionary: ISM manufacturing stayed above 50 for a second month, ISM services accelerated sharply to 56.1, new orders and backlogs improved, and the services complex pushed back against immediate recession narratives. But the labor side weakened materially by week-end, with ADP showing narrow sector-led hiring and non-farm payrolls printing a large downside surprise, negative revisions, higher unemployment, and broader sector softness beyond strike effects.
Inflation remained the binding complication. Manufacturing prices surged to 70.5, services cost pressure stayed persistent, and wage growth remained firm even as payroll momentum softened. Productivity helped absorb part of compensation growth, but not enough to deliver a clean disinflationary labor-cost signal. The resulting regime is not a clean growth break and not a clean reacceleration: services demand is still holding, labor deterioration is becoming visible, and the Fed’s easing optionality reopens only conditionally because the inflation gate remains unresolved.
B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD
Growth: +
Supporting evidence:
ISM Services rose to 56.1, the strongest supplied activity signal of the week, with new orders at 58.6, employment back above 50, and backlogs expanding for the first time in 12 months. ISM Manufacturing stayed in expansion at 52.4 for a second month despite a mild loss of momentum in new orders and production. The weekly growth signal was therefore still supportive, led by services rather than manufacturing.
Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
ADP showed only moderate hiring at +63k and narrow sector breadth, concentrated in education/health and construction. The NFP report then delivered the decisive deterioration signal: payrolls fell -92k versus +59k expected, prior months were revised down by -69k, unemployment rose to 4.4%, household employment fell, and JGM sector notes pointed to weakness across housing/social services, construction, transport, and leisure/hospitality. Strike and weather distortions diluted signal purity, but not enough to dismiss the labor-softening impulse.
Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
The ISM Manufacturing Prices Index surged to 70.5, up 11.5pp m/m, reintroducing a clear input-cost concern. ISM services commentary also pointed to persistent cost pressure, while NFP wage dynamics remained firm despite weaker employment. Productivity growth helped contain unit labor costs, but compensation growth at +5.7% and ULC at +2.8% were still not benign enough to remove the inflation constraint.
Financial Conditions: +
Supporting evidence:
The supplied tape notes that rate markets reacted to the payroll shock by raising the probability of policy easing later in the year, with yields declining and the USD weakening after NFP. That is an easing impulse in market financial conditions. But the move was conditional because inflation confirmation was still required before a clean policy shift.
Policy Constraint: ~
Supporting evidence:
The labor side reopened easing optionality: the NFP miss, negative revisions, and unemployment rise weakened the Fed’s employment side. But ISM price pressure, persistent services costs, and still-firm wages kept the inflation gate binding. The result is neither a clean easing signal nor a renewed hawkish-growth signal; it is data-dependent optionality, with CPI/PCE confirmation required.

Professional Market Commentary
Institutional commentary interpreted the report as a clear downside labor surprise with several temporary distortions.

• Negative revisions: prior months revised lower by ~69K, weakening the recent hiring trend.
• Unemployment rate increase: move to 4.4% interpreted as early evidence of labor-market loosening.

Comment — Economist Layer
The headline payroll decline is too weak to ignore, even after adjusting for strike effects.
Key elements:
• Strike distortion: roughly 28–31K healthcare jobs temporarily removed.
• Downward revisions: -69K across prior two months.
• Unemployment rate: increased to 4.4%, confirming gradual labor loosening.
However the deterioration appears broader than the strike alone.
JGM notes highlight sectoral weakness:
Housing & Social Services: -18.6K
Construction: -14K
Transport: -11K
Leisure & Hospitality: -27K
This breadth argues that labor demand is softening across several cyclically sensitive sectors.
Weather effects may have distorted the household survey, but payroll weakness is still visible in the establishment data.
Participation dynamics also matter:
• participation rate declined
• population growth remained strong
• household employment fell ~185K
These elements reinforce the signal of labor-market fragility rather than a purely statistical miss.
Wage dynamics complicate the picture.
Average Hourly Earnings increased roughly 0.4% m/m (~3.7–3.8% YoY), leaving the economy in a partial stagflation configuration:
softening employment momentum but still-firm wage growth.
JGM note:
January payroll strength may have overstated the true state of the labor market, with February representing partial payback.
Birth-death model effects also appear relevant:
• positive contribution in January
• reversal in February
Participation changes across age groups remain important.
Prime-age participation remains historically high, but demographic shifts in older cohorts continue to influence the aggregate labor supply.

DATA WINDOW: 9 Feb 2026 to 24 Feb 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The combined February window resolved into a demand-bending but not labor-breaking regime. Retail and housing delivered the clearest weakening signals: the corrected Feb 10 retail block showed a control-group miss, existing home sales fell sharply, NAHB weakened, new home sales missed, and Case-Shiller confirmed slower home-price momentum. Against that, industrial production and Philly Fed manufacturing surprised positively, and the post-CPI window still showed enough manufacturing / industrial support to avoid a clean growth-break conclusion.

Labor and inflation kept the Fed in conditional-cuts mode rather than urgent easing. NFP beat on the headline, unemployment fell to 4.3%, and claims stayed broadly stable, even if the labor quality signal was composition-tainted and hiring remained narrow. Inflation progress was incomplete: import prices and ECI helped the benign-cost narrative, but CPI core at 0.3% m/m, firm wages, and sticky services framing kept March easing off the table. FOMC minutes reinforced data dependence. The resulting regime is cuts later and conditional: restrictive policy is bending demand through consumption and housing, but labor is not weak enough and inflation is not clean enough to force near-term Fed relief.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
The demand side weakened through the corrected Feb 10 retail control miss, existing home sales at 3.91M with an -8.4% m/m decline, NAHB at 36, new home sales at 615k, and slower Case-Shiller price growth. Industrial production rose 0.7% m/m and Philly Fed manufacturing surprised at 16.3, so the window did not confirm a synchronized growth break. But the dominant household / housing impulse was negative, with manufacturing acting as offset rather than leadership.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
NFP rose +130k versus +70k expected, private payrolls beat, unemployment fell to 4.3%, and the workweek improved. But composition was debated, healthcare/social assistance looked heavy, wages remained firm, and claims showed only a subdued-but-stable labor market rather than renewed labor tightness. Labor stabilized enough to block urgent cuts, but did not cleanly reaccelerate.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Import prices were benign at +0.1% m/m and ECI cooled to +0.7% q/q, supporting the wage / external-cost moderation channel. CPI headline softened, but core CPI printed +0.3% m/m, the source tape framed services as sticky, and AHE in the payroll report rose +0.4% m/m. No new inflation print in the post-CPI portion reset the regime. Inflation progress remained incomplete.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Housing showed the clearest restrictive financial-conditions transmission. Existing home sales fell sharply, NAHB declined, new home sales missed, and Case-Shiller slowed, with repeated references to mortgage rates above roughly 7%, affordability constraints, lock-in effects, and builder incentives. The real-economy financial-conditions impulse was restrictive through housing and major purchases.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The window reinforced March hold / later conditional cuts. The retail and housing data increased growth sensitivity to disinflation confirmation, but NFP and wages removed labor urgency, CPI core at 0.3% was too sticky for immediate easing, and FOMC minutes emphasized a data-dependent hold. The Fed was not pushed toward hikes, but the bar for near-term cuts remained high.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 2 Feb 2026 to 6 Feb 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week widened the US manufacturing-versus-labor divergence. Manufacturing broke sharply higher, with ISM moving back into expansion for the first time in 12 months, new orders surging to 57.1, and S&P Global confirming expansion. Services also remained expansionary, but with slower momentum and higher prices. The growth signal was therefore not recessionary; the issue was quality and durability, because the manufacturing bounce was explicitly contaminated by January reorder effects and tariff front-running risk.

Labor became the dominant downside signal. ADP was near stall speed, jobless claims jumped with weather distortion, and JOLTS delivered the clearest deterioration: openings fell to 6.542M, the lowest since 2020, with broad weakness across professional services, retail and finance and a quit rate stuck at 2.0%. Inflation did not give the Fed clean relief: ISM manufacturing prices rose to 59.0, services prices paid rose to 66.6, and 5-year Michigan inflation expectations edged higher even as 1-year expectations fell. The weekly regime is therefore manufacturing false dawn versus labor-demand deterioration, with the Fed pulled between rising easing optionality and still-binding inflation risk.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
ISM Manufacturing jumped to 52.6 from 47.9, with new orders at 57.1, and S&P Global Manufacturing confirmed expansion at 52.4. ISM Services remained expansionary at 53.8, with business activity at 57.4, while S&P Global Composite at 53.0 stayed consistent with roughly positive core demand. But services momentum moderated, new orders in services slowed, and manufacturing strength was potentially distorted by January reorder effects and tariff front-running. Growth improved tactically, but durability was unresolved.

Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
ADP printed only 22k versus 48k expected, confirming weak hiring momentum. Claims rose to 231k from 212k, with weather distortion likely but still a break from recent low-claims stability. The decisive release was JOLTS: openings fell to 6.542M versus 7.200M expected, the lowest since 2020, with sharp declines in professional/business services, retail and finance, and the quit rate frozen at 2.0%. Labor demand deterioration became visible.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
ISM Manufacturing Prices Paid rose to 59.0, while ISM Services Prices Paid accelerated to 66.6 from 64.3. Services price pressure re-emerged despite moderating demand, reinforcing the prior pipeline-inflation concern. Michigan 1-year inflation expectations fell to 3.5%, which helped at the margin, but 5-year expectations rose to 3.4%, leaving the inflation constraint unresolved.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
The supplied tape did not provide a decisive direct financial-conditions release. The macro mix implies two-way pressure: labor deterioration and weaker JOLTS support easing optionality, while better manufacturing and firmer price components argue against aggressive easing. Financial conditions therefore remained unresolved inside the window.

Policy Constraint: ~
Supporting evidence:
The labor side became more dovish through ADP, claims and especially JOLTS, but inflation pressure in manufacturing and services prices prevented a clean easing signal. The week did not validate a hawkish growth regime because labor demand weakened materially, but it also did not justify aggressive cuts because price pressure remained visible. The Fed remained in hold / verification mode.

MONDAY 2 FEB 2026

US — ISM Manufacturing PMI (Jan)
Release Date: 2 Feb 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Actual: 52.6 vs Consensus: 48.5 vs Prior: 47.9 — MAJOR BEAT
New Orders: 57.1 vs Prior: 47.4
Employment: 48.1 vs Prior: 44.9
Prices Paid: 59.0 vs Prior: 58.5

Professional Market Commentary

Majority view: first expansion in 12 months marked a significant shift. New orders at the highest since February 2022 signaled a genuine demand rebound, according to major US banks and ISM official commentary.

Minority view: January reorder month and tariff front-running were likely distorting the signal; sustainability was uncertain until confirmed by production data.

Key debate: structural manufacturing recovery or tactical pre-tariff stockpiling.

ISM Chair Spence official statement: “Although these are positive signs for start of year, they are tempered by commentary citing January is a reorder month after holidays, and some buying appears to be to get ahead of expected price increases due to ongoing tariff issues”

Comment — Economist Layer

Dominant signal: Manufacturing broke a 12-month contraction streak with an explosive rebound. New orders surged to 57.1 from 47.4, making this the cleanest demand signal rather than just an inventory rebuild.

Secondary signal: Employment still contracted at 48.1 despite activity expansion. Labor shedding continued even as orders improved, consistent with productivity-driven efficiency gains.

Noise: January seasonality and tariff front-running contaminated the signal. Professional consensus flagged sustainability risk.

State transition: improvement / possible false dawn.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Reaction-function impact: marginally hawkish on the growth tail because the release reduced recession probability, but the Fed likely discounts part of the move as temporary. Employment weakness keeps the labor-market cooling narrative intact.

Transmission: If sustained, the signal passes into the capex / production chain over 3–6 months. If tariff-driven, it creates inventory-overhang risk by Q2.

US — JOLTS Job Openings (Dec)
Release Date: 5 Feb 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Actual: 6.542M vs Consensus: 7.200M vs Prior: 7.146M (revised to 6.928M) — MAJOR MISS

Professional Market Commentary

Majority view: Job openings fell to the lowest since September 2020. Labor demand cooled sharply, especially in professional/business services (-257k) and retail (-195k), according to Indeed Hiring Lab and major economists.

Quit rate was stuck at 2.0%, with workers “frozen” in place and unable to move for better pay. KPMG Chief Economist Swonk described this as “gut-wrenching for middle class.”

Key concern: openings fell below the number of unemployed persons, around 7.5M, for the first time since 2017 outside the pandemic.

Debate: “bending not breaking” labor market versus “uncomfortably close to breaking point,” per Indeed Hiring Lab.

BLS official data: professional/business services -257k, retail -195k, finance -120k, showing concentrated weakness.

Comment — Economist Layer

Dominant signal: labor-demand collapse. Openings at 6.542M, the lowest in more than four years and 658k below consensus, are not noise; they reflect a genuine hiring freeze.

Secondary signal: Quit rate frozen at 2.0%, versus pre-pandemic around 2.3–2.4%, shows workers trapped and mobility dead. The low-hire / low-fire regime is confirmed.

Noise: JOLTS lags because December data was released on February 5, but the magnitude of the miss and breadth of sectoral weakness across professional services, retail and finance make this first-order.

State transition: deterioration — labor demand collapse / hiring-freeze confirmation.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Reaction-function impact: dovish. The release supports easing optionality and raises the probability of a March or May cut if the trend continues.

Transmission: Weak openings pass into slower hiring, weaker wage pressure and cooler services inflation with a 6–9 month lag.

<<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 26 Jan 2026 to 30 Jan 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week showed softer underlying momentum than headline growth optics implied. Durable goods, factory orders, and Chicago PMI beat, but the quality of the signal was uneven: orders were volatile, Dallas manufacturing stayed weak, CFNAI softened, confidence rolled over, and the goods trade deficit widened sharply. Claims stayed benign but did not prove labor strength, because the more relevant late-cycle framing remained low-hire / low-fire rather than renewed labor tightness.

The binding weekly shift was inflation. PPI re-accelerated meaningfully, especially core PPI, reviving pipeline inflation risk and raising the probability that core PCE would not cool cleanly. The Fed held as expected, and the data mix supported a plateau / patient stance rather than imminent cuts: growth was not breaking, labor was not firing-led weak, but confidence and trade softened while inflation risk reappeared.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Durable goods beat strongly at +5.3% m/m, core capex rose +0.7%, factory orders rose +2.7%, and Chicago PMI jumped to 54.0. But CFNAI fell to -0.42, consumer confidence missed sharply at 84.5, Dallas manufacturing remained weak at -10.9, and goods trade deteriorated to -56.8B. The week therefore showed hard-goods resilience but weaker breadth and softer confidence, not clean re-acceleration.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Initial claims stayed low at 209k and continuing claims were better than expected at 1.827M, but the source explicitly cautioned that low claims should be treated as seasonal / post-holiday layoff behavior rather than true labor strength. The week did not include a decisive hiring release, so the labor read remained low-hire / low-fire rather than tightening or breaking.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
PPI was the dominant adverse release. Final demand rose +0.5% m/m versus +0.2% expected, core PPI rose +0.7% m/m versus +0.2%, and core y/y rose to 3.3% versus 2.9% expected. Housing prices remained positive, reducing confidence in a rapid housing-led disinflation impulse. Pipeline inflation risk therefore reappeared.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
The supplied tape did not include a decisive broad financial-conditions release. Housing price data stayed positive but not booming, while confidence weakness and trade drag pointed to softer activity. The FOMC hold and PPI upside leaned against imminent easing, but there was no standalone evidence of a sharp tightening or easing impulse.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The FOMC hold was expected, but the week’s data mix raised the bar for fast cuts. <<USD_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 19 Jan 2026 to 23 Jan 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week reinforced a softer-than-headline early-2026 momentum regime rather than a clean acceleration story. Consumption was still firm in the November PCE bundle, PMIs remained expansionary, and claims stayed low, but the quality of the data was fragile: pending home sales collapsed, leading indicators remained negative, construction was choppy, PMIs pointed to only around 2% core-demand growth, and the consumption run-rate was increasingly funded by thin household buffers.

Inflation was compatible with cautious easing but not urgent easing. Core PCE held at 0.2% m/m and around a 2.8% y/y plateau, while Michigan inflation expectations improved and energy inventories were disinflationary at the margin. But factory-gate price pressure in the PMI introduced a near-term core-goods risk, and labor data did not show a firing cycle despite claims nuance. The resulting regime is Fed patience: cautious easing remains viable later, but the week did not force a pivot.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Personal consumption remained firm, with nominal consumption at +0.5% m/m and real consumption at +0.3% m/m. S&P Global composite PMI held at 52.8, consistent with positive but not accelerating core demand. Against that, pending home sales fell -9.3% m/m, the Leading Index declined -0.3%, construction spending was noisy across September and October, and PMI forward indicators softened. Growth was still positive, but the forward impulse was softer than consensus acceleration narratives implied.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Initial claims stayed very low at 200k, and continuing claims fell to 1.849M versus 1.900M expected. But the source explicitly reframed low claims as seasonal / flow mechanics rather than true labor strength, and PMIs showed composite employment barely above neutral and below historical averages. The week therefore truncated the imminent firing-cycle tail without proving labor re-acceleration.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Core PCE printed +0.2% m/m and 2.8% y/y, compatible with cautious easing but still around a plateau rather than a clean return to target. Michigan inflation expectations improved, with 1-year expectations down to 4.0% and 5-year expectations down to 3.3%, while energy inventories were marginally disinflationary. But PMI factory-gate inflation reached a five-month high, creating a near-term core-goods CPI risk.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
MBA mortgage rates eased slightly to 6.16%, but levels remained restrictive, and pending home sales collapsed to 71.8 with a -9.3% m/m decline. The source framed housing as highly rate- and affordability-elastic, with the housing-floor story fragile when rates back up even modestly. Financial conditions therefore remained restrictive through the housing turnover channel.

Policy Constraint: ~
Supporting evidence:
The PCE data supported cautious easing compatibility, but not urgency. Growth and labor were not weak enough in stress terms to force a pivot, while PMI price signals introduced some risk of core-goods inflation stickiness. The reaction-function message was Fed patience: medium-term easing remains viable if services prices normalize and consumption cools, but the week did not force near-term action.

Mildly dovish at the margin, mainly through lower inflation expectations. Not enough to force a Fed shift because core PCE and labor remain more important.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Consumption and PMIs remained expansionary, but the support base was fragile: saving buffers were thin, pending home sales collapsed, leading indicators stayed negative, and PMI forward components softened.

Why it matters: The week does not validate a recession call, but it also undercuts an H1 acceleration narrative.

Implication: Fed patience remains the correct reaction-function read: cautious easing is compatible with the data, but not urgent without labor stress or cleaner inflation normalization.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

Personal Income / Consumption / PCE (22 Jan 2026)
Why it mattered: This was the core regime bundle. Consumption stayed firm and core PCE was stable, but spending was supported by thin buffers and inflation remained around a plateau.

S&P Global Flash PMI (23 Jan 2026)
Why it mattered: PMIs confirmed expansion, but only around a 2% core-demand pace, with softer forward momentum and mixed price signals.

Why it mattered: The -9.3% m/m collapse was the clean downside surprise and preserved housing as the clearest rate-sensitive transmission channel.

DATA WINDOW: 12 Jan 2026 to 16 Jan 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week reinforced a soft-landing-with-friction regime rather than a clean reacceleration. Consumption held up through retail control, existing home sales bounced, industrial production surprised positively, and claims remained very low, but the quality of the expansion stayed constrained: real income was not booming, saving buffers were thin, small-firm capex expectations remained depressed, and manufacturing survey strength still required hard-data confirmation given tariff front-running and seasonality risks.

Inflation was softer in CPI micro but less clean in Fed-reaction-function terms. Core CPI surprised lower and goods / shelter details supported the disinflation path, while Cleveland Fed CPI and trade-services margin compression reinforced the benign goods read. But the CPI-to-PCE mapping looked less dovish, with possible mechanical firmness in core PCE, food-away-from-home pressure, and import-price strength complicating the “inflation solved” narrative. The weekly policy message was Fed patience: low layoffs and resilient consumption reduce urgency to cut, while softer CPI micro keeps later easing alive if disinflation persists.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Retail control rose +0.4% m/m, existing home sales beat at 4.35M, industrial production rose +0.4% m/m, and manufacturing output rose +0.2% m/m versus -0.2% expected. But NFIB capex intentions remained weak, real weekly earnings were still soft, Redbook slowed, and some hard-activity strength may reflect tariff front-running / stockpiling rather than durable momentum. Growth held up, but the impulse was not broad enough to call reacceleration.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Employment Trends improved to 105.80 and initial claims fell to 198k, with continuing claims down to 1.884M, confirming the economy was not entering a firing cycle. But the source repeatedly framed the regime as low layoffs / low hiring, with unemployment risk able to drift through weak hiring and entrants rather than firing. Labor remained stable, not tight.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Core CPI surprised softer at +0.2% m/m versus +0.3% expected, core goods were flat, shelter was slowing, and Cleveland Fed CPI eased to +0.1%. But the source’s CPI-to-PCE mapping warned that core PCE could print firmer mechanically, food away from home rose +0.7% m/m, import prices rose +0.4% m/m, and tariff/pass-through pressure was partly absorbed in margins rather than disappearing. Inflation improved in CPI micro but did not deliver a clean policy-clearing signal.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
MBA 30-year mortgage rates eased to 6.18% from 6.25%, supporting the housing bounce, and existing home sales rose sharply. But borrowing costs remained high, small-firm investment stayed constrained, and NAHB remained depressed at 39. Financial conditions eased at the margin but remained restrictive enough to cap housing and capex upside.

Policy Constraint: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week did not materially shift the Fed out of patience mode. Softer CPI micro and low tariff pass-through support the later-easing path, but low claims, resilient consumption, stronger industrial production, and potentially firmer core PCE mapping give the Fed room to wait. The distribution tightened around hold now, cut later only if disinflation persists and hiring remains soft.

Release Date: 13 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Headline CPI m/m: Actual +0.3% vs Cons +0.3% vs Prior +0.3%

Core CPI y/y: Actual 2.6% vs Cons 2.7% vs Prior 2.6%

Professional Market Commentary

No separate institutional tape was supplied beyond the user-integrated CPI micro. The release was treated as a neutral headline CPI print but a softer core CPI surprise versus consensus.

Comment — Economist Layer

Headline is neutral; the week’s inflation signal is in the core micro and CPI-to-PCE mapping, not the headline.

First-order core signal: the downside core surprise versus consensus supports a softer underlying inflation narrative once paybacks are stripped out. This directly interacts with the regime anchor from the previous week’s productivity / ULC data: cost-side relief is increasingly doing the disinflation work even with services activity not collapsing.

Core goods were flat; core goods ex-auto showed only a muted rebound at +0.2%. Used vehicles fell -1.1% and new vehicles were flat, meaning goods pricing still behaves like a normalization / margin-absorption story, not a new inflation wave.

Tariff pass-through framing matters: just over half passed through, with the rest absorbed. This implies goods inflation can stay contained even with policy noise because pressure first shows up in margins before CPI.

Core services rose +0.3%, but composition is crucial. Airfares at +5.2% and accommodation at +2.9% were framed as mechanical payback after shutdown-related discounting. Underlying discretionary-demand indicators cited in the source — passengers flat y/y and hotel occupancy slightly lower — argue against a demand-led services reacceleration.

Health-insurance methodology drag and lower motor-vehicle repair costs muted core services. This makes the print soft but messy.

Shelter: rent / OER in a slowing trend at 0.26 / 0.31; new-lease rent trackers soft, with Zillow at 2.3% y/y. That reinforces the pipeline-disinflation story, with the usual CPI-shelter noise caveat.

CPI-to-PCE mapping: this is the non-trivial part of the week. The source mapping suggests core PCE for December could be firmer than core CPI, around 0.38–0.40% m/m pending delayed PPI inputs, partly through PCE weights / components and food services. This creates a near-term inference trap: CPI says “underlying softer,” while PCE may print “firmer” mechanically.

Food / real-income angle: food away from home at +0.7% m/m is a PCE-relevant pressure point and a real-income tax. It matters more for consumption endurance than for immediate Fed reaction unless it persists and bleeds into expectations.

State transition: disinflationary improvement in CPI micro, but PCE-mapping risk remains.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Policy transmission: the CPI-to-PCE mapping complicates a clean dovish read into the next FOMC — not because inflation is reaccelerating, but because the Fed’s preferred measure could look stickier even if the micro is benign.

This keeps the Fed in patient / verification mode rather than giving markets a clean near-term easing trigger.

DATA WINDOW: 5 Jan 2026 to 9 Jan 2026

A. US WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week tightened the soft-landing regime rather than breaking it. Manufacturing remained in contraction and goods-sector orders / capex were still soft, but services activity re-accelerated meaningfully, S&P Global services confirmed expansion, claims stayed low, and unemployment fell after shutdown distortions reversed. Labor cooled through slower payroll growth, narrow hiring breadth and higher continuing claims, but the data did not validate a firing-cycle or hard-landing labor break.

Inflation risk was two-sided. Manufacturing prices paid stayed elevated and wages remained resilient, but the decisive offset was productivity: Q3 productivity surprised sharply higher and unit labor costs fell, reducing the risk that firm services demand automatically translates into persistent wage-price pressure. The Fed reaction function therefore stayed in patience mode: near-term aggressive easing became harder to justify after stronger services, but productivity-led disinflation capped the hawkish tail and preserved a later-cut path.

B. US MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: +
Supporting evidence:
ISM services rose to 54.4 versus 52.3 expected, with new orders at a 15-month high and employment back in expansion. S&P Global composite PMI printed 53.0, services PMI 52.9, and productivity surprised strongly. Against that, ISM manufacturing remained in contraction at 47.9, factory orders / durables confirmed goods softness, and consumer credit disappointed. Growth was therefore services-led and still expansionary, even if goods remained a drag.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
ADP showed subdued hiring at +41k, JOLTS openings cooled to 7.15m, NFP slowed to +50k, and hiring breadth was narrow, with goods, retail, construction and professional services contracting. But initial claims stayed low at 208k, unemployment fell to 4.38%, and services employment rebounded in ISM. The labor signal was cooling without collapse.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
ISM manufacturing prices paid remained elevated at 58.5 and wages stayed resilient, with AHE at +0.33% m/m and 3.76% y/y. But ISM services prices paid fell to an 8-month low around 64.3, productivity surged, and unit labor costs fell -1.9%, materially reducing wage-price spiral risk. Inflation pressure was not gone, but the cost-side offset improved.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
No broad financial-conditions release dominated the week. Housing remained rate-constrained, consumer credit disappointed, and goods-sector softness persisted, but low claims, resilient services, and productivity strength limited stress. The supplied file does not show a decisive economy-wide easing or tightening impulse.

Policy Constraint: ~
Supporting evidence:
The Fed was validated in patience. Stronger services and low claims made aggressive near-term easing harder to justify, while productivity-led unit-labor-cost relief capped the hawkish tail. The policy distribution compressed around hold longer, cut later; March easing remained possible but no longer assured.

Release Date: 6 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

S&P Global Composite PMI: Actual 53.0 vs Cons 52.7
S&P Global Services PMI: Actual 52.9 vs Cons 52.5

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The releases were treated as confirming expansionary services-led activity and offsetting weak ISM manufacturing.

Comment — Economist Layer

Composite activity remained expansionary, reinforcing the soft-landing base case and offsetting weak ISM manufacturing. Services remained in expansion, confirming that growth momentum was services-led.

State transition: continuation of services-led expansion.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Supports patience in policy pricing. Services expansion reduces the probability that goods weakness alone triggers a dovish pivot.

WEDNESDAY 7 JAN 2026

US — ADP National Employment (Dec)
Release Date: 7 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Actual +41k vs Cons ~50k vs Prior revised -29k

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The release was treated as subdued but positive hiring, with services gains dominating and goods employment continuing to contract.

Comment — Economist Layer

Hiring was positive but subdued, consistent with late-cycle normalization. Composition matters: services gains dominated, while goods employment continued to contract.

State transition: continuation of slow-hiring regime.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

ADP mainly frames expectations into NFP rather than shifting policy probabilities by itself. The release supports cooling without collapse.

US — Durables / Factory Orders (Oct, revised)
Release Date: 7 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: **

Reuters Data

No mechanical values supplied.

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The release was treated as confirmation of capex and goods softness, with some underlying stabilization in ex-transport and non-defense capex.

Comment — Economist Layer

Headline durables and factory orders confirm capex and goods softness, but ex-transport and non-defense capex show underlying stabilization rather than collapse. This caps recession risk but does not generate upside torque.

State transition: continuation of goods softness with stabilization beneath the headline.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Growth-negative at the margin for the goods-rebound story, but not recession-confirming. The release keeps goods-sector drag in place while avoiding a collapse signal.

US — ISM Non-Manufacturing PMI / Prices Paid (Dec)
Release Date: 7 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

ISM Non-Manufacturing PMI: Actual 54.4 vs Cons 52.3 vs Prior 52.6
ISM Services Prices Paid: Actual ~64.3, 8-month low

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The release was the dominant weekly signal: services activity re-accelerated meaningfully, reaching a 13-month high with broad-based strength.

Comment — Economist Layer

Services activity re-accelerated materially. New orders surged to a roughly 15-month high, rebuilding forward momentum into early 2026. Employment rebounded into expansion, directly countering narratives of imminent labor-market stress.

Sequential strength was partially distorted by post-shutdown normalization, so cross-survey confirmation matters more than one-month acceleration.

Prices paid were directionally constructive for disinflation, falling to an 8-month low, but the level remained consistent with elevated supercore PCE. Inflation pressure is easing at the margin, not normalized.

State transition: improvement in services activity, with inflation pressure easing but not normalized.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This materially raises the confidence threshold for easing and truncates the near-term dovish tail. The price component supports hold longer rather than early cuts, because disinflation is directional but incomplete.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Dovish at the margin for labor, but not enough to dominate the week because services activity was strong and claims stayed anchored.

THURSDAY 8 JAN 2026

US — Q3 Productivity / Unit Labor Costs (prelim)
Release Date: 8 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Productivity: Actual +4.9% annualized vs Cons +3.0% vs Prior +4.1%
Unit labor costs: -1.9%

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The release was framed as a critical macro offset: productivity growth surprised sharply to the upside, lifting the 12-month pace to around 1.9%, well above the pre-COVID norm. Non-financial corporate productivity also strengthened.

Professional macro interpretation increasingly viewed this as structural efficiency gains, plausibly linked to technology / AI adoption rather than pure cyclical noise.

Comment — Economist Layer

This matters because unit labor costs fell 1.9%, a powerful disinflationary signal for supercore services. Productivity gains allow services growth without commensurate wage inflation, materially capping the hawkish tail.

State transition: disinflationary improvement through productivity / ULC.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This print offsets strong ISM services and is the key reason the Fed can remain patient without re-tightening bias. It supports later easing optionality even with firm services activity.

US — International Trade (Oct)
Release Date: 8 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Actual -$29.4bn vs Prior -$48.1bn

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The headline improvement was described as distorted by volatile categories.

Comment — Economist Layer

Headline improvement is distorted. Exports were boosted by non-monetary gold, excluded from GDP, while imports fell sharply due to a temporary collapse in pharmaceutical shipments linked to tariff announcements.

Ex-distortions: net trade likely provides a mechanical boost to Q4 GDP, but imports, notably tech-related, appear to be re-accelerating into year-end, consistent with ongoing AI-related investment.

State transition: improvement in headline trade balance, but distortion-heavy.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Policy relevance is limited. This is a GDP-tracking input, not a regime signal.

US — Initial Jobless Claims / Continuing Claims
Release Date: 8 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Initial Jobless Claims: Actual 208k vs Cons 210k
Continuing Claims: Actual 1.91m

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. Claims were treated as firmly anchored, while continuing claims drifted higher.

Comment — Economist Layer

Claims remain firmly anchored. This is the cleanest real-time labor signal and directly contradicts recessionary narratives.

Continuing claims gradually drifted higher, consistent with rebalancing rather than stress.

State transition: continuation of low-firing labor regime.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Claims reduce the probability of imminent labor stress and support Fed patience. Continuing claims keep the low-hire / rebalancing nuance alive.
