FRIDAY 9 JAN 2026

US — Non-Farm Payrolls / Unemployment / Wages / Hours (Dec)
Release Date: 9 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Non-Farm Payrolls: Actual +50k vs Cons +70k (revised)
Unemployment Rate: Actual 4.38% vs Prior 4.54%
AHE: +0.33% m/m; 3.76% y/y
Average workweek: 34.2

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied. The release was framed as materially slower payroll growth, with prior months revised down, but not recessionary. Services job gains were concentrated in leisure / hospitality and healthcare, while goods, retail, construction and professional services contracted.

Comment — Economist Layer

Payroll growth slowed materially and prior months were revised down. On a headline basis this is soft, but not recessionary.

Composition matters: services job gains were concentrated in leisure / hospitality and healthcare, while goods, retail, construction, and professional services contracted. Diffusion indices remained just above 50, signaling narrow and uneven hiring.

The unemployment-rate decline reflected higher household employment and partial reversal of shutdown-related distortions. This invalidates a hard-landing labor narrative, even as payroll growth slows.

Underlying job growth estimates suggest hiring near or slightly below breakeven, implying cooling without collapse.

Wage growth remained resilient, but productivity gains materially offset inflation risk. The Fed will place greater weight on ECI and unit labor costs, which now point to easing pressure by mid-year.

State transition: labor cooling without collapse.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The report reinforces the week’s core labor regime: cooling but not deteriorating. It does not justify aggressive near-term cuts because unemployment fell and claims stayed low, but it prevents a renewed labor-overheating narrative.

Release Date: 9 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Professional Market Commentary

Comment — Economist Layer

Starts weakened, particularly multifamily, while permits held up, suggesting flooring rather than renewed downside. Housing remains rate-constrained, with limited macro spillover.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Housing is still constrained by rates but not collapsing. Secondary for the Fed unless housing weakness broadens into consumption or labor.

US — University of Michigan Sentiment / Inflation Expectations (Jan prelim)
Release Date: 9 Jan 2026 | Local Time: not provided
Importance: **

Reuters Data

No mechanical values supplied.

Professional Market Commentary

No detailed institutional tape was supplied.

Comment — Economist Layer

Sentiment improved modestly and inflation expectations remained contained. No de-anchoring signal.

State transition: improvement in sentiment, expectations contained.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Mildly constructive, but secondary to hard labor and inflation data. It supports the view that inflation psychology is not worsening.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Services activity re-accelerated strongly, but labor was cooling and goods/manufacturing remained soft, while productivity sharply reduced the inflation risk from stronger services demand.

Why it matters: The week made aggressive near-term easing harder to justify but also capped the hawkish tail through lower unit labor costs.

Implication: The Fed is validated in patience: hold longer, cut later if disinflation persists, with March easing possible but not assured.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

ISM Non-Manufacturing PMI / Prices Paid (7 Jan 2026)
Why it mattered: This was the dominant growth signal. Services activity re-accelerated to 54.4 with stronger new orders and employment, while prices paid eased but stayed elevated.

Q3 Productivity / Unit Labor Costs (8 Jan 2026)
Why it mattered: Productivity at +4.9% and ULC at -1.9% were the decisive inflation offset, allowing stronger services demand without a full hawkish policy repricing.

Non-Farm Payrolls / Unemployment / Wages (9 Jan 2026)
Why it mattered: Payroll growth slowed and breadth narrowed, but unemployment fell and wages stayed resilient, preserving cooling-without-collapse rather than a labor-break regime.
